<dc:title>The general circulation of the atmosphere : 2000 program in Geophysical Fluid Dynamics</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Salmon, Rick</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Held, Issac M.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Fields, Janet</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Thiffeault, Jean-Luc</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Dynamics</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Atmosphere</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Circulation</dc:subject>
<dc:description>In its millenial session, the GFD program focused on "The General Circulation of the Atmosphere." Isaac Held led the charge with an inspiring two-week introductory course, strongly supported by the lectures of Alan Plumb, Wayne Schubert, Michael Cullen, and Oliver Buhler.  As the dust settled, the important concept of pseudomomentum shone brightly in the sky above Walsh Cottage. This is a concept which has virtually transformed meteorology within the past 20 years, yet it remains mostly unfamiliar to oceanographers and others.  Thus our "pseudomomentum summer" perfectly fit the GFD mission of diffusing ideas across disciplinary boundaries.  As the summer wore on, the range of topics broadened in its customary way to include: the general circulation of Jupiter and Mars; lattice-Boltzmann and gas-kinetic methods for solving the equations of fluid mechanics; the scattering of sound waves; the heating of the universe; and many more topics too numerous to list.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the Office of Naval Research under Contract No. N00014-97-1-0934 and the National Science Foundation under Contract No. OCE-9810647.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Conceptual models of the climate : 2002 program of study, Bounds on turbulent transport</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Whitehead, John A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Busse, Friedrich</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Howard, Louis</dc:creator>

<dc:subject>Turbulent transport</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>convection</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Planetary observations</dc:subject>
<dc:description>The subject of "Bounds of Turbulent Transport" was introduced in a series of ten lectures. The six lecturers constitute almost all the contributors to this subject.  The subject was introduced and foundations laid by five lectures by F. H. Busse. In the middle of the first week, L. Howard reviewed his historical first approach to this subject  and described more recent advances.  Additional lectures by P. Constantine, R. Kerswell, C. Caulfield and C. Doering provided modern advances. We trust that the lecture notes will constitute a timely review of this promising subject.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the Office of Naval Research under contract number N00014-97-1-0934 and The National Science Foundation under contract number OCE 98-10647.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Conceptual models of the climate : 2003 program of study, Non-Newtonian geophysical fluid dynamics</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Balmforth, Neil J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Hinch, John</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Fluids</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Flow</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Non-Newtonian</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Non-Newtonian fluids occur commonly in our world. These fluids, such as&#xd;
toothpaste, saliva, oils, mud and lava, exhibit a number of behaviors that are&#xd;
different from Newtonian fluids and have a number of additional material&#xd;
properties. In general, these differences arise because the fluid has a&#xd;
microstructure that influences the flow. In section 2 we will present a collection of&#xd;
some of the interesting phenomena arising from flow nonlinearities, the inhibition&#xd;
of stretching, elastic effects and normal stresses. In section 3 we will discuss a&#xd;
variety of devices for measuring material properties, a process known as&#xd;
rheometry.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Deployment of the northern fish cage and mooring, University of New Hampshire — Open Ocean Aquaculture Program summer 2000</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Irish, James D.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Paul, Walter</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Ostrom, William M.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Chambers, Michael</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Fredriksson, David W.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Stommel, Matt</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Aquaculture</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Mooring</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Fish cage</dc:subject>
<dc:description>The University of New Hampshire - Open Ocean Aquaculture (UNH-OOA) program has worked for the past few years on developing the technology to deploy and maintain fish cages in open, exposed northern waters. In June 1999, two Sea Station octagonal net cages by Ocean Spar Technologies were deployed with their UNH designed and constructed moorings.  In June 2000 the Northern Cage and its mooring were retrieved, examined and repaired , and readied for redeployment. This was a complex operation, initiated by a team of UNH  ocean engineers lead by Dr. Barbaros Celikkol. This year's effort was expanded with the addition of a Program Manager (Michael Chambers), the Fishing Vessel Nobska, and researchers from the Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution (WHOI). During the week of 21 to 25 August 2000, the cage and mooring were assembled and deployed at the UNH-OOA site seven miles offshore the New Hampshire coast, south of the Isle of Shoals.  This collaborative effort involved members of  the UNH Mechanical Engineering Dept., UNH divers, members of the WHOI Applied Ocean Physics &amp; Engineering Dept. and the Captain and crew of the FV Nobska. Ship support for the deployment was provided by the R/V Gulf Challenger and Galen J. (UNH) and the FV Nobska (a 100 foot fishing vessel based at Woods Hole, MA). The work was favored by light wind and sea conditions. The endeavor resulted in the successful placement of the North Cage and its complex mooring system with load cells and environmental sensors. Unexpected and unexplained tangling of the mooring system, in particular near its grid corner points, was encountered and corrected.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Fudning was provided by the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Adminstration for the Open Ocean Aquaculture Project under Contract No. NA86RG0016 to the Univesity of New Hampshire and under Subcontracts 00-394 and 01-442 to the Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Turbulence in the shallow nearshore environment during SANDYDUCK '97</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Fredericks, Janet J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Trowbridge, John H.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Voulgaris, George</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Turbulence</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Nearshore</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>SANDYDUCK</dc:subject>
<dc:description>An array of five acoustic Doppler velocimeters (ADV), which produce high quality measurements of the three-dimensional velocity vector in a sample volume with a scale of one centimeter, was deployed from late August through late November of 1997 at a water depth of approximately 4.5 m off Duck, North Carolina. The sensors were deployed near the sea floor but above the centimeters-thick wave boundary layer, and the sampling scheme was designed to resolve turbulence statistics averaged over tens of minutes, much longer than typical wave periods but shorter than time scales associated with variablity of energetic wind-driven and wave-driven alongshore flows.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Science Foundation under Grant No. OCE-9810609, the Mellon Foundation and Rinehart Coastal Research  Center.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Loose-tube neutral tether fiber optic termination procedure</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Bowen, Martin F.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Fiber-optics</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Tether</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Procedure</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Neutral tethers for unmanned underwater vehicles may contain a bundle of single-mode, optical fibers that are protected inside&#xd;
flexible stainless steel tubing. To date the author knows of no commercially available systems or kits that substitute for the&#xd;
following procedure, which is a step-by-step method for terminating electro-optical, loose-tube tether assemblies when used in&#xd;
oil-compensated, underwater applications. This procedure alone will not result in a load-bearing assembly. It assumes that the&#xd;
tether's outer jacket and synthetic strain-relief layer have already passed through and been terminated to an unspecified, customized,&#xd;
load-bearing assembly. The procedure addresses in detail the preparation of three optical fibers and three copper conductors for&#xd;
repeated make-and-break installations into a vehicle's primary junction box. The user will need a large, clean workbench, plenty of&#xd;
light and a variety of specialized tools, most of which are listed. A spreadsheet is provided that describes the parts required and&#xd;
suggests appropriate vendors or other sources. The entire procedure can take up to four hours to complete per tether end.</dc:description>

</metadata></record><record><header><identifier>oai:darchive.mblwhoilibrary.org:1912/21</identifier><datestamp>2016-03-08T19:00:49Z</datestamp><setSpec>com_1912_4</setSpec><setSpec>col_1912_10</setSpec><setSpec>col_1912_554</setSpec></header><metadata><oai_dc:dc xmlns:oai_dc="http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc/" xmlns:doc="http://www.lyncode.com/xoai" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xmlns:dc="http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/" xsi:schemaLocation="http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc/ http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc.xsd">
<dc:title>Fluid mechanical measurements within the bottom boundary layer during coastal mixing and optics</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Fredericks, Janet J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Trowbridge, John H.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Williams, Albert J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Voulgaris, George</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Shaw, William J.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Bottom boundary layer</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Stress</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Dissipation</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Oceanus (Ship : 1975-) Cruise</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Endeavor (Ship: 1976-) Cruise</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Seward Johnson (Ship) Cruise</dc:subject>
<dc:description>To quantify and understand the role of vertical mixing processes in determining mid-shelf vertical structure of hydrographic and optical properties and particulate matter, the Office of Naval Research (ONR) funded a program called Coastal Mixing and Optics (CMO), which was conducted at a mid-shelf location in the Mid-Atlantic Bight, south of Martha's Vineyard, Massachusetts. As part of the CMO program, a tall tripod, called 'SuperBASS,' was equipped to collect a year-long, near-bottom time-series of velocity, temperature, salinity and pressure. The BASS sensors were modified to measure absolute as well as differential acoustic travel time, to provide sound speed (a surrogate for temperature) and velocity in a single sample volume. Seven BASS velocity and time travel sensors were placed between 0.4 and 7 meters above bottom. Three acoustic Doppler velocity (ADV) meters were mounted near the bottom-most BASS sensors at 0.3 meters above bottom. The sensors were used to obtain high-quality time-series measurements of velocity and temperature throughout a large fraction of the bottom bondary layer on the New England shelf. The measurements provide vertical structure of the Reynolds-averaged velocity and temperature fields, direct covariance estimates of turbulent Reynolds stress and turbulent heat flux, and indirect inertial range estimates of dissipation rate for turbulent kinetic energy and temperature variance. The purpose of this report is to describe the SuperBASS instrumentation and deployments, to provide summaries of the data collected, and to document the processing, preliminary analysis and archival of data collected for this component of the program.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the Office of Naval Research under contract number N00014-95-1-0373.</dc:description>
<dc:date>2005-07-15T18:59:06Z</dc:date>

<dc:title>Spatial variability of bottom turbulence over a linear sand ridge mooring deployment and AUTOSUB AUV survey cruise report  R/V RRS Challenger, cruise number 146 Broken Bank, North Sea, U.K., 17 – 28 August 1999 cruise report</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Voulgaris, George</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Trowbridge, John H.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Terray, Eugene A.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Bottom turbulence</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Acoustics</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Currents</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Challenger (Ship) Cruise 146</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Two successful AUTOSUB deployments were carried out during August 1999 as part of the AUTOSUB&#xd;
Thematic Program project titled “Spatial Variability of Bottom Turbulence over a Linear Sand Ridge,” funded&#xd;
by the Natural Environment Research Council (NERC), U.K. The AUTOSUB Autonomous Underwater Vehicle&#xd;
(AUV) was deployed and used to survey flow patterns at a location near the Broken Bank, southern North Sea,&#xd;
U.K. The AUV was equipped with acoustic flow and turbulence sensors and its surveys aimed at mapping the&#xd;
spatial variation of flow and turbulence near the bed and over topographic features.&#xd;
Three instrumented bottom mounted frames were also deployed, around the AUV survey area, for a period of&#xd;
approximately 5 days. The purpose of this array was to gather information on the temporal variability of the flow&#xd;
and turbulence near the seabed and to identify the important terms that drive circulation around the bank.&#xd;
Additional data were gathered including CTD casts, seabed samples and acoustic images of the seabed (side-scan&#xd;
sonar).&#xd;
The purpose of this data collection was to help identify the flow patterns around ridges and to understand the&#xd;
mechanisms controlling the maintenance and evolution of such features.&#xd;
This report describes the operations carried out by researchers from the University of South Carolina, Woods&#xd;
Hole Oceanographic Institution, Southampton Oceanography Centre and the AUTOSUB Team on the R.V. RRS&#xd;
Challenger during the period 17th –28th August 1999.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the Office of Naval Research under Contract No. N00014-01-10255 and the Natural Environment Research Council, UK Award GST/02/2155 to the University of Southampton.</dc:description>
<dc:date>2005-07-15T19:11:31Z</dc:date>
<dc:date>2005-07-15T19:11:31Z</dc:date>

<dc:title>Preliminary acoustic and oceanographic observations from the ASIAEX 2001 South China Sea Experiment</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Newhall, Arthur E.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Costello, Lawrence</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Duda, Timothy F.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Dunn, James M.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Gawarkiewicz, Glen G.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Irish, James D.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Kemp, John N.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>McPhee, Neil M.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Liberatore, Stephen P.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Lynch, James F.</dc:creator>

<dc:creator>Trask, Richard P.</dc:creator>

<dc:subject>Shallow water acoustics</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>ASIA EX/South China Sea</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Coastal oceanography</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Ocean Researcher 1 (Ship) Cruise 609</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Ocean Researcher 1 (Ship) Cruise 610</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Ocean Researcher 1 (Ship) Cruise 611</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Ocean Researcher 3 (Ship) Cruise 651</dc:subject>
<dc:description>The Asian Seas International Experiment (ASIAEX) was a very successful scientific collaboration between the United States of&#xd;
America (USA), the People’s Republic of China (PRC), Taiwan (ROC), the Republic of Korea (ROK), Japan, Russia, and&#xd;
Singapore. Preliminary field experiments associated with ASIAEX began in spring of 2000. The main experiments were performed&#xd;
in April-August, 2001. The scientific plan called for two major acoustics experiments, the first a bottom interaction experiment in&#xd;
the East China Sea (ECS) and the second a volume interaction experiment in the South China Sea (SCS). In addition to the&#xd;
acoustics efforts, there were also extremely strong physical oceanography and geology and geophysics components to the&#xd;
experiments. This report will concentrate on describing the moored component of the South China Sea portion of ASIAEX 2001&#xd;
performed from the Taiwan Fisheries research vessel FR1 (Fisheries Researcher 1). Information on the environmental moorings&#xd;
deployed from the Taiwanese oceanographic research vessel OR1 (Oceanographic Researcher 1) will also be listed here for&#xd;
completeness, so that the reader can pursue later analyses of the data. This report does not pursue any data analyses per se.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the Office of Naval Research under Grant Numbers&#xd;
N00014-01-1-0772, N00014-98-1-0413 and N00014-00-1-0206.</dc:description>

<dc:title>4DGeoBrowser : a web-based data browser and server for accessing and analyzing multi-disciplinary data</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Lerner, Steven A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Maffei, Andrew R.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Multi-discipline data access</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Geospatial</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Web data browser</dc:subject>
<dc:description>This report describes the 4DGeoBrowser software system. The GeoBrowser is a web-based application developed at the Woods&#xd;
Hole Oceanographic Institution by Steven Lerner and Andrew Maffei. It has been designed with the goal of creating, accessing, and&#xd;
analyzing repositories of oceanographic datasets that have been generated by investigators in differing scientific disciplines. Once&#xd;
the information is loaded onto a Geobrowser server the investigator-user is able to login to the website and use a set of data access&#xd;
and analysis tools to search, plot, and display this information. GeoBrowser servers are also capable of processing commands that&#xd;
are submitted remotely via HTTP URLs or email. Scientists are able to use this capability to make calls to the GeoBrowser server&#xd;
and generate click-able maps, tables of urls, and customized HTML pages. These can then be used to enhance websites associated&#xd;
with scientific projects. Examples of supporting scientific website functionality that includes time series plotting, data delivery by&#xd;
email, geo-spatial plotting of interdisciplinary data, map-based search capabilities and other functionality are presented in this&#xd;
report. The report includes examples of GeoBrowser application websites, a user manual, and a reference guide. In addition, the&#xd;
concept of Electronic Index Cards (EICs) is presented.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Instrumentation for open ocean aquaculture monitoring</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Irish, James D.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Carroll, Megan</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Singer, Robin C.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Newhall, Arthur E.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Paul, Walter</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Johnson, Craig</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Witzell, Nick</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Rice, Glen</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Fredriksson, David W.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Open ocean aquaculture</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Motion monitoring</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Mooring line tension</dc:subject>
<dc:description>The Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution is assisting the University of New Hampshire by instrumenting a fish cage and mooring as part of their Open Ocean Aquaculture demonstration program in the Gulf of Maine. To understand these systems, the wave and current forcing and the response of the mooring and fish cage needs to be measured. A UNH mooring with an ADCP measured the current forcing. Tension in the mooring lines was measured by load cells deployed with the mooring during servicing in August 2000. Load cells were placed in each anchor line, and, in the NE corner, also in the two grid lines and the riser line to the fish cage. Low power recording systems were deployed on the load cell mounting bars by divers on 22 October 2000, recorded good data through January 2001, when they were turned around and redeployed. Three single load cell recorders were recovered in July 2001 and recorded though 23 June when their data storage filled. The four load cell system was recovered in March after a large winter storm, and had failed in early March. The wave forcing was measured with a wave rider buoy with a 3-axis accelerometer measuring its motion. The acceleration was integrated twice to obtain wave displacement. The system  mooring contained a compliant elastic. The wave rider was deployed on 4 January 2001 and recovered on 17 March 2001 after a major Northeast storm.  It recorded data throughout its deployment. The motion of the moored fish cage was measured by a motion package constructed around a 6-axis Motion-Pak and a PC-104 data system. The motion package was deployed on the fish cage from Jan into March 2001 and recorded motions thoughout without difficuly. It observed a major storm in early March where the counter weight was lost from the fish cage, and its increase in motion thereafter.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Fudning was provided by National Oceanic and Atmospheric Adminstration for the Open Ocean Aquaculture Project under Contract No. NA86RG0016 to the University of New Hampshire and under Subcontracts 00-394 and 01-442 to the Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Shearmeter floats in the area of the WHOI Brazil Basin Tracer Release Experiment : technical and oceanographic data</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Duda, Timothy F.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Guest, Brian J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Wooding, Christine M.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Jones, Clayton M.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Lelievre, Scott</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Webb, Douglas C.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Shear</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Abyssal currents</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Finestructure</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Knorr (Ship : 1970-) Cruise KN161</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Six drifting floats designed to measure shear were deployed in the vicinity of the Brazil Basin Tracer Release Experiment. The&#xd;
one-year long time series of oceanographic conditions obtained by the floats are for direct comparison with long-term tracer&#xd;
dispersion. The purpose of the tracer dispersion experiment was to study mixing of Antarctic Bottom Water at approximately 4000&#xd;
m depth with less dense water above. Two of the floats returned shear records, one from about 1660 m depth and one from about&#xd;
2800 m depth. Mean shear at 1660 m was 2.2 x 10 -3 s-1 with N = 1.1 cph, about 1.9 times the Garrett-Munk model amount.&#xd;
Mean shear at 2800 m was 1.1 x 10-3 with N = 0.5 cph, about 2.2 times Garrett-Munk. There was no apparent depth structure to&#xd;
the shear recorded by the near-bottom float moving over the mountainous seafloor. The two shear time series and the local tidal&#xd;
velocities were not strongly correlated, but the tide and shear series did have some similarities. Some variability in the 1660-m&#xd;
shear may be due to atmospheric forcing. Three floats deeper than 2800 m returned one-year long trajectories. Two trajectories were&#xd;
persistently eastward.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Science Foundation under&#xd;
Grant Nos. OCE-9416014 and OCE-9906685.</dc:description>

<dc:title>The Jason II virtual control van system, data acquisition system, web-based event logger, and SeaNet</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Lerner, Steven A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Maffei, Andrew R.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Multi-sensor data access and display</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Metadata and data acquisition</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>ROV Jason</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>SeaNet</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Scientific underwater remotely operated vehicles (ROVs) collect data from multiple video cameras and scientific instruments. This&#xd;
integrated information is often only available in an ROV control-van during operations. Although all the data is logged, it is&#xd;
difficult for scientists to re-create a combined display of this data and have the ability to review and access an entire cruise dataset&#xd;
easily. We introduce a methodology of taking continuous real-time information snapshots (infosnaps) during interesting events and&#xd;
at regular time intervals for complete data coverage. These infosnaps capture four simultaneous video sources, vehicle data,&#xd;
instrument data, and event data as entered by scientists. The infosnaps are automatically cataloged and immediately accessible and&#xd;
searchable via a web-browser. We developed, built, and deployed the Jason II Virtual Control Van system on seven Jason cruises.&#xd;
The system has captured over 50,000 control-van infosnaps, containing more than 200,000 images co-registered with vehicle&#xd;
telemetry and scientific instrument data. The Virtual Control Van is designed for both scientific collaboration and&#xd;
public/educational outreach. It has been integrated with the SeaNet system to provide remote on-shore access. The report describes&#xd;
the Jason II Virtual Control Van system and includes instructions for setting up the system in the field.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the W. M. Keck Foundation under Grant No. 991735.</dc:description>
<dc:date>2005-07-15T20:23:00Z</dc:date>

</metadata></record><record><header><identifier>oai:darchive.mblwhoilibrary.org:1912/28</identifier><datestamp>2016-03-08T16:34:06Z</datestamp><setSpec>com_1912_4</setSpec><setSpec>col_1912_10</setSpec><setSpec>col_1912_554</setSpec></header><metadata><oai_dc:dc xmlns:oai_dc="http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc/" xmlns:doc="http://www.lyncode.com/xoai" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xmlns:dc="http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/" xsi:schemaLocation="http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc/ http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc.xsd">
<dc:title>Turbulence in the coastal environment during HYCODE</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Fredericks, Janet J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Trowbridge, John H.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Turbulence</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Stress</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>HYCODE</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Endeavor (Ship: 1976-) Cruise EN342</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Endeavor (Ship: 1976-) Cruise EN344</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Endeavor (Ship: 1976-) Cruise EN347</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Endeavor (Ship: 1976-) Cruise EN356</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Endeavor (Ship: 1976-) Cruise EN358</dc:subject>
<dc:description>A tall tripod equipped with two acoustic Doppler velocimeters (ADVs) was deployed at a water depth of 15 m off the coast of New&#xd;
Jersey near the LEO-15 site. Sensors were co-located near the bottom to provide good estimates of Reynolds stress. Thermistors&#xd;
were located within several centimeters of the velocity sample volume to provide simultaneously sampled estimates of turbulent&#xd;
temperature variance and vertical temperature flux. One of the ADVs was equipped with a pressure and a temperature sensor. A&#xd;
wave/tide gauge was placed at 4 meters above bottom. The instruments were deployed late July through early December of 2000 and&#xd;
late June through early August of 2001. For the 2001 deployment, a single beam acoustic Doppler velocity sensor (DopBeam) was&#xd;
added to measure high frequency vertical velocity variance and echo intensity within the bottom boundary layer.&#xd;
A second tripod was deployed nearby and was equipped with an array of LISST sensors and an MSCAT.&#xd;
The purpose of this report is to document the instrumentation and deployment of the tripods and to document the tall tripod data by&#xd;
providing a description of the processing and data formats, time-series summaries of the burst averaged data along with preliminary&#xd;
analyses.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the Office of Naval Research under Contract No. N00014-99-1-0213.</dc:description>

</metadata></record><record><header><identifier>oai:darchive.mblwhoilibrary.org:1912/29</identifier><datestamp>2016-03-08T18:57:39Z</datestamp><setSpec>com_1912_4</setSpec><setSpec>col_1912_5</setSpec><setSpec>col_1912_554</setSpec></header><metadata><oai_dc:dc xmlns:oai_dc="http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc/" xmlns:doc="http://www.lyncode.com/xoai" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xmlns:dc="http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/" xsi:schemaLocation="http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc/ http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc.xsd">
<dc:title>Numbers of calling whales in the North Pacific</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Watkins, William A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Daher, Mary Ann</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>George, Joseph E.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Numbers of calling whales</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>North Pacific whales</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>SOSUS arrays monitor whales</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Since November 1995, the U. S. Navy's Sound Surveillance System (SOSUS) and other hydrophone arrays were used to regularly sample the occurance of whale sounds in the four regions bordering the continental margins across the North Pacific. The numbers of whales heard calling varied with the season and location for each species, Blue whales (Balaenoptera musculas), fin whales (Balaenoptera physalus), and humpback whales (Megaptera novaeangliae). For blue whales, calling during the fall season averaged 5 whales per event, winter averaged 1.5 whales per event, spring averaged 1 whale, and summer averaged 1.5 whales. For fin whales the number of whales heard ("F" calls from individuals) during winter averaged 3 whales per event, spring and fall calling averaged 1.5 whales, and summer averaged 1 whale. The "J" calling events, regardless of season, were judged to be from at least 6 fin whales.  Humpback singing typically was from 3 whales. These numbers demonstrated seasonal variations in calling whales for each region.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by CNO N45 Environmental Program and US Army Corps of Engineers&#xd;
(DCA87-00-H-0026) with funding from the Department of Defense&#xd;
Legacy Resource Management Program.</dc:description>

</metadata></record><record><header><identifier>oai:darchive.mblwhoilibrary.org:1912/30</identifier><datestamp>2016-03-08T18:54:03Z</datestamp><setSpec>com_1912_4</setSpec><setSpec>col_1912_8</setSpec><setSpec>col_1912_554</setSpec></header><metadata><oai_dc:dc xmlns:oai_dc="http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc/" xmlns:doc="http://www.lyncode.com/xoai" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xmlns:dc="http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/" xsi:schemaLocation="http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc/ http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc.xsd">
<dc:title>Eddys in the Arctic Ocean from IOEB ADCP data</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Krishfield, Richard A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Plueddemann, Albert J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Honjo, Susumu</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Arctic halocline eddys</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Upper ocean currents</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Drifting buoy observations</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Filtered and Earth-referenced ADCP data from the B92, B97 and S97 IOEBs were demodulated to remove inertial and near-inertial&#xd;
tidal frequencies, in order to highlight the low frequency components for examination of Arctic submesoscale eddys. This report&#xd;
describes the raw data, processing scheme, and numerical and graphical results of this analysis, which are also available at&#xd;
http://ioeb.whoi.edu/ioebeddys.htm. Using the demodulated timeseries of current profiles from each buoy, characteristics of 95&#xd;
possible eddy encounters are quantified by (1) identifying anomalously large velocities associated with subsurface vortices, (2)&#xd;
determining the vortex centers and their drift, and (3) determining vortex properties as a function of radius and depth. Out of 44&#xd;
total months of observations, 81 of the encounters were determined to be subsurface eddies, and 29 were eddy core encounters.&#xd;
Only 14 of the confirmed subsurface encounters were cyclonic, versus 66 anticyclonic, and one indeterminate. Within the southern&#xd;
and central Canadian basin portion of the Beaufort Gyre, halocline eddys with maximum velocities between 10 and 45 cm/s,&#xd;
centered around 140 m depth, and over 100 m thick were prevalent. Over the Northwind Ridge, eddy encounters were absent from&#xd;
any timeseries. Farther north and west over the Chukchi Cap, encounters resumed, but were generally smaller, more shallow and&#xd;
less intense (although these observations were mostly derived from a lower resolution transmitted data subset).</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Science Foundation Grant No. OPP-9815303,&#xd;
and by the Office of Naval Research Grant No. N00014-97-1-0135.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Long-term evolution and coupling of the boundary layers in the Stratus Deck Regions of the eastern Pacific (STRATUS)</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Lucas, Lisanne E.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Way, Bryan S.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Weller, Robert A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Bouchard, Paul R.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Fischer, Albert S.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Moffat, Carlos F.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Schneider, Wolfgang</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Fewings, Melanie R.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Stratocumulus clouds</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Air-sea interaction</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Moored data</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Melville (Ship) Cruise Cook 2</dc:subject>
<dc:description>A surface mooring was deployed in the eastern tropical Pacific west of northern Chile from the R/V Melville as part of the Eastern Pacific Investigation of Climate (EPIC). EPIC is a CLIVAR study with the goal of investigating links between sea surface temperature variability in the eastern tropical Pacific and climate over the American continents. Important to that goal is an understanding of the role of clouds in the eastern Pacific in modulating atmosphere-ocean coupling. The mooring was deployed near 20°S 85°W, at a location near the western edge of the stratocumulus cloud deck found west of Peru and Chile. This deployment started a three-year occupation of that site by a WHOI surface mooring in order to collect accurate time series of surface forcing and upper ocean variability. The surface mooring was deployed by the Upper Ocean Processes Group of the Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution (WHOI). In collaboration with investigators from the University of Concepcion, Concepcion, Chile, an XBT section was made on the way out to the mooring from Arica, Chile, and an XBT and CTD section was made on the way into Arica.&#xd;
The buoy was equipped with meteorological instrumentation, including two Improved METeorological (IMET) systems. The mooring also carried Vector Measuring Current Meters, single-temperature recorders, and conductivity and temperature recorders located in the upper meters of the mooring line.  In addition to the instrumentation noted above, a variety of other instruments, including an acoustic current meter, an acoustic doppler current profiler, a bio-optical instrument package, and an acoustic rain guage, were deployed.&#xd;
This report describes, in a general manner, the work that took place and the data collected during the Cook 2 cruise aboard the R/V Melville. The surface mooring deployed during this cruise will be recovered and re-deployed after approximately 12 months and again after 24 months, with a final recovery planned for 36 months after the first setting.  Details of the mooring design and preliminary data from the XBT and CTD sections are included.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration&#xd;
under grant number NA96GP0429.</dc:description>

<dc:title>North Brazil Current Rings Experiment : mooring S1 data report, November 1998 - June 2000</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Glickson, Deborah A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Fratantoni, David M.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Tropical Atlantic circulation</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Mesoscale rings</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Temperature</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Seward Johnson (Ship) Cruise</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Nineteen months of temperature and salinity data were recovered from North Brazil Current (NBC) Rings Experiment Mooring S1.&#xd;
The mooring, located east of Barbados at 13º 00’N, 57º 53’W between November 1998 and June 2000, consisted of a vertical array&#xd;
of five temperature/conductivity recorders, five temperature recorders, one 150 kHz acoustic Doppler current profiler (ADCP), and&#xd;
one 260 Hz RAFOS sound source. This instrumentation was distributed over a depth interval (500-1100m) coincident with the&#xd;
low-salinity core of Antarctic Intermediate Water. Due to low concentration of scattering particles at 1000 m, the ADCP failed to&#xd;
return useful velocity data. Heading, pitch, and roll data were successfully recorded, however, and provide coarse measurement of&#xd;
current intensity. Four anomalously low temperature, low salinity, and (inferred) high-velocity events appear toward the end of the&#xd;
record. The temperature and salinity fluctuations observed during these events are most likely due to a combination of vertical&#xd;
instrument excursions due to current-induced mooring tilt and advection of anomalous NBC ring-core water past the mooring site.&#xd;
Anomalous conditions persist for a period of 2-3 weeks and appear, based on simultaneous surface drifter trajectories and satellite&#xd;
ocean color observations, to be associated with the passage of NBC Rings near Barbados.</dc:description>

<dc:title>The Northwest Tropical Atlantic Station (NTAS) : NTAS-1 mooring deployment cruise report</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Plueddemann, Albert J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Galbraith, Nancy R.</dc:creator>

<dc:subject>Oceanus (Ship : 1975-) Cruise OC365</dc:subject>
<dc:description>A surface mooring outfitted for meteorological and oceanographic measurement was deployed near 14°50'N, 51°00'W in the&#xd;
northwest tropical Atlantic on 30 March 2001. This was the initial deployment of the Northwest Tropical Atlantic Station (NTAS)&#xd;
project for air–sea flux measurement. These observations will be used to investigate air–sea interaction processes related to climate&#xd;
variability. The deployment was done on R/V Oceanus Cruise 365, Leg 5 by the Upper Ocean Processes Group (UOP) of the&#xd;
Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution. The 3-meter discus buoy was outfitted with two Air–Sea Interaction Meteorology&#xd;
(ASIMET) systems. Each system measures, records, and transmits via Argos satellite the surface meteorological variables necessary&#xd;
to compute air–sea fluxes of heat, moisture and momentum. The upper 120 m of the mooring line was outfitted with oceanographic&#xd;
sensors for the measurement of temperature and velocity. This report describes the initial deployment of the NTAS mooring&#xd;
(NTAS-1), including some of the pre-cruise buoy preparations and post cruise data comparisons.</dc:description>

<dc:title>The 1995 Georges Bank Stratification Study and moored array measurements</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Alessi, Carol A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Beardsley, Robert C.</dc:creator>

<dc:subject>U.S. GLOBEC</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Moored array</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Parizeau (Ship) Cruise PAR94-018</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Parizeau (Ship) Cruise PAR95-010</dc:subject>

<dc:subject>Endeavor (Ship: 1976-) Cruise EN259</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Endeavor (Ship: 1976-) Cruise EN260</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Endeavor (Ship: 1976-) Cruise EN262</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Endeavor (Ship: 1976-) Cruise EN269</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Endeavor (Ship: 1976-) Cruise EN271</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Endeavor (Ship: 1976-) Cruise EN274</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Seward Johnson (Ship) Cruise SJ95-04</dc:subject>

<dc:description>The 1995 Geoges Bank Stratification Study (GBSS) was the first intensive process study conducted as part of the U.S. GLOBEC Northwest Atlantic/Georges Bank field program. The GBSS was designed to investigate the physical processes which control the seasonal development of stratification along the southern flank of Georges Bank during spring and summer. Past work suggested that during this period, larval cod and haddock tended to aggregate to the thermocline on the  southern flank where higher concentrations of their copepod prey were found. A moored array was deployed as part of GBSS to observe the onset and evolution of sesonal stratification  over the southern flank with sufficient vertical and horizontal resolution that key physical processes could be identified and quantified. Moored current, temperature, and conductivity (salinity) measurements were made at three sites along the southern flank, one on the crest, and one on the northeast peak of the bank. Moored surface meteorological measurements were also made at one southern flank site to determine the surface wind stress and heat and moisture fluxes. The oceanographic and meteorological data collected with the GBSS array during January-August 1995 are presented in this report. Meteorological data collected on National Data Buoy Center environmental buoys 44011 (Georges Bank), 44008 (Nantucket Shoals), and 44005 (Gulf of Maine) are included in this report for completeness and comparison with the GBSS southern flank meteorological measurements.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Science Foundation under Grant Numbers&#xd;
OCE-98-06379 and OCE-98-06445.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Warm water pathways in the northeastern North Atlantic ACCE RAFOS float data report November 1996 - November 1999</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Furey, Heather H.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Bower, Amy S.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Richardson, Philip L.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>North Atlantic Circulation</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Subpolar region</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Floats</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Knorr (Ship : 1970-) Cruise KN147</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Knorr (Ship : 1970-) Cruise KN151</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Knorr (Ship : 1970-) Cruise KN154</dc:subject>
<dc:description>This is the final data report of all acoustically tracked RAFOS float data collected by the Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution in&#xd;
1996-1999 during the Atlantic Climate Change Experiment (ACCE). The RAFOS float component of ACCE, entitled "Warm&#xd;
Water Pathways and Intergyre Exchange in the Northeastern North Atlantic," was designed to measure the warm water currents&#xd;
entering the northeastern North Atlantic which become the source of intermediate and deep waters in the subpolar region. The&#xd;
experiment was comprised of three RAFOS float deployments on the R/V Knorr: the first in fall 1996 along the continental slope&#xd;
seaward of Porcupine Bank, the second in spring 1997 along the mid-Atlantic Ridge, and the final deployment in fall 1997 along&#xd;
both the Ridge and the Bank. Seventy floats were deployed, 13 RAFOS and 2 ALFOS in fall 1996, 14 RAFOS in spring 1997,&#xd;
and 41 RAFOS in fall 1997. The isobaric ALFOS floats were ballasted for 800 decibars and were launched to monitor the regions'&#xd;
sound sources during the experiment. The RAFOS floats were isopycnal and ballasted for the 27.5 sigma-t surface to target the&#xd;
intermediate-depth North Atlantic and Poleward Eastern Boundary Currents. The objectives of the Lagrangian float study were (1)&#xd;
to provide a quantitative description of the bifurcation of the North Atlantic Current east of the Mid-Atlantic Ridge, (2) to assess&#xd;
the importance of meridional eddy fluxes, compared to large-scale advection, in the northward flux of heat and salt in the&#xd;
northeastern North Atlantic, and (3) to establish the degree of continuity of the Poleward Eastern Boundary Current as it flows to&#xd;
the entrance of the Norwegian Sea and the fate of the Mediterranean Outflow Water carried by this current.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Science Foundation under Grant Number OCE-9831877.</dc:description>

<dc:title>A compact coastal ocean observing system for Kernel Blitz 2001</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Gobat, Jason I.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Weller, Robert A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Way, Bryan S.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Lord, Jeffrey</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Pritchard, Mark</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Smith, Jason C.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Moored observatories</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Data telemetry</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Tactical oceanography</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>New Horizon (Ship) Cruise</dc:subject>
<dc:description>In this report we describe a compact, easily deployed, moored system for oceanographic and meteorological observations in the&#xd;
coastal ocean. The system consists of a surface and subsurface mooring pair deployed adjacent to one another. Compared to a single&#xd;
catenary surface mooring, this arrangement allows the entire water column to be instrumented. All of the instruments in the system&#xd;
log high resolution time series data. Additionally, the mooring line instruments periodically report averaged data to the buoys via&#xd;
inductive modems. On the subsurface mooring, this averaged data is sent to the surface buoy using an acoustic modem. Inductively&#xd;
coupled mooring line instrumentation includes conductivity, temperature, and pressure sensors, acoustic current meters, and optical&#xd;
backscattering and absorption sensors. In addition to mooring line instruments, the surface buoy collects averaged data from&#xd;
meteorological sensors, including wind speed and direction, barometric pressure, relative humidity, air temperature, precipitation,&#xd;
longwave and shortwave radiation, sea surface temperature and conductivity, and wave height and period. Data from both mooring&#xd;
lines and from the surface meteorological sensors is telemetered to shore via line-of-sight radio and satellite. The entire system,&#xd;
including buoys, moorings, instruments, launch and recovery gear, telemetry receive, and data processing facilities can be packed&#xd;
into a single 20 foot shipping container. The system was successfully deployed to provide environmental monitoring for Kernel&#xd;
Blitz 2001, a US Navy fleet exercise off southern California. Results from the deployment are presented.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the Office of Naval Research under Contract Number N000149910090.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Long-term evolution of the coupled boundary layers (STRATUS) mooring recovery and deployment cruise report NOAA Research Vessel R H Brown • cruise RB-01-08 9 October - 25 October 2001</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Vallee, Charlotte</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Weller, Robert A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Bouchard, Paul R.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Ostrom, William M.</dc:creator>

<dc:subject>Ronald H. Brown (Ship) Cruise RB01-08</dc:subject>
<dc:description>This report documents the work done on cruise RB-01-08 of the NOAA R/V Ron Brown. This was Leg 2 of R/V Ron Brown’s&#xd;
participation in Eastern Pacific Investigation of Climate (EPIC) 2001, a study of air-sea interaction, the atmosphere, and the upper&#xd;
ocean in the eastern tropical Pacific. The science party included groups from the Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution (WHOI),&#xd;
NOAA Environmental Technology Laboratory (ETL), the University of Washington (UW), the University of California, Santa&#xd;
Barbara (UCSB), and the University Nacional Autonoma de Mexico (UNAM). The work done by these groups is summarized in this&#xd;
report. In addition, the routine underway data collected while aboard R/V Ron Brown is also summarized here.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration under&#xd;
Grant Numbers NA96GPO429 and NA17RJ1223.</dc:description>

<dc:title>CBLAST-Low 2001 pilot study mooring deployment cruise and data report ; FV Nobska, June 4 to August 17, 2001</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Pritchard, Mark</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Gobat, Jason I.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Ostrom, William M.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Lord, Jeffrey</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Bouchard, Paul R.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>CBLAST-LOW</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Low wind</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Air-sea interaction</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Nobska (Ship) Cruise</dc:subject>
<dc:description>During the summer of 2001, several moorings and cruises were used as part of the CBLAST-Low (Coupled Boundary Layer Air-Sea&#xd;
Transfer under low wind conditions) pilot experiment in the North Atlantic, south of Martha’s Vineyard Island, MA, USA. Six&#xd;
subsurface tide gauges were deployed around the study site for a period of approximately 3 months during the summer of 2001.&#xd;
Further, two surface buoys equipped with meteorological instrumentation and subsurface arrays that measured temperature,&#xd;
conductivity and velocity were deployed during the months of July and August 2001. For a short intensive operating period during&#xd;
July 2001, a newly manufactured three-dimensional mooring designed to sample three-dimensional properties of the upper ocean was&#xd;
deployed for a period of 6 days. During the Intensive Operating Period (IOP) along-shelf and across-shelf&#xd;
conductivity-temperature-depth (CTD) sections were completed as well as a drifting array designed to passively collect data from the&#xd;
upper water column released for approximately 24 hours. This report describes the instrumentation and type of moorings deployed by&#xd;
the Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution Upper Ocean Processes (WHOI UOP) group as well as data return and quality from the&#xd;
CBLAST-Low 2001 pilot study. This is summarized in graphical and tabular form in this report.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding provided by the Office of Naval Research under Contract No. N00014-01-1-0029&#xd;
and from the Secretary of the Navy / CNO Chair Grant No. N00014-99-1-0090.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Use of the High Resolution Profiler (HRP) in the Salt Finger Tracer Release Experiment (SFTRE)</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Montgomery, Ellyn T.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Salt-fingers</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Thermohaline staircase</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Diffusive mixing</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Oceanus (Ship : 1975-) Cruise OC365</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Seward Johnson (Ship) Cruise SJ01-12</dc:subject>
<dc:description>The Salt Finger Tracer Release Experiment (SFTRE) was conducted in the tropical North Atlantic&#xd;
in 2001. The experimental area was east of Barbados and is characterized by thermohaline&#xd;
staircase features prevalent in the depth range of 200−600 meters. The goal of this experiment was&#xd;
to quantify the distribution and intensity of vertical mixing in a region of thermohaline staircases.&#xd;
Two cruises were required to accomplish this goal: one to survey with the High Resolution Profiler&#xd;
(HRP) and inject sulfur hexafluoride (SF6) tracer, and another ten months later to map the spatial&#xd;
distribution of tracer and obtain additional estimates of diffusive and turbulent mixing rates using&#xd;
the HRP.&#xd;
The first cruise of the SFTRE experiment took place between January 15 and February 12, 2001&#xd;
on the R/V Oceanus, leg 365-2 (OC365). An XBT survey identified an area of robust staircases&#xd;
that became the injection site. Then 175 kg of SF6 tracer was injected in nine streaks in a layer&#xd;
with temperature of about 10°C. When the injection mechanism was being replenished, HRP&#xd;
profiles were made in the area of the tracer patch. The profiles yielded estimates of the mixing&#xd;
rates at the start of the experiment. Near the end of the cruise, water samples from the patch were&#xd;
used to map the actual tracer distribution immediately after deployment.&#xd;
The second cruise occurred between October 29 and December 4 on the R/V Seward Johnson, leg&#xd;
01−12 (SJ0112). Its objective was to sample and map the vertical and horizontal distribution of&#xd;
tracer after ten months. The work completed included 172 CTD casts with chemical analysis&#xd;
performed on the water samples, and 165 HRP profiles. Despite covering an area of 500,000&#xd;
nautical miles², only 50−60% of the tracer was found, suggesting higher than expected lateral&#xd;
mixing.&#xd;
The SFTRE included the deployment of a Moored Profiler. The profiles acquired by the MP provide&#xd;
background on the temporal variation of the temperature, salinity, and velocity fields where it was&#xd;
deployed. To share costs of personnel, the MP was deployed and recovered on cruises that followed&#xd;
ours, in conjunction with other mooring activities. The MP was deployed in February 2001 from&#xd;
R/V Oceanus and recovered by the R/V Knorr in April 2002.&#xd;
The program was a success, despite not fully delimiting the tracer distribution, because the&#xd;
observations allow more complete quantification of the mixing processes occurring in this region.&#xd;
The inferred mixing intensity was stronger and the influence of the thermohaline staircases more&#xd;
widespread than initially expected.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding provided by the National Science Foundation under Grant No. OCE-0081502.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Recognizing problems in shipboard logging meteorology systems</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Payne, Richard E.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Shipboard meteorology</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Sensor problems</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>UNOLS ships</dc:subject>
<dc:description>This report is to assist shipboard technicians whose responsibilities are maintaining digital logging meteorological systems. Examples of good and bad data recorded on ships by IMET (Improved METeorology) systems are given, as well as general comments on how various parameters should appear when plotted against time.&#xd;
&#xd;
Parameters included are: wind speed and direction, air temperature, relative humidity, barometric pressure, short- and long-wave radiation, and precipitation (R. M. Young gauges).</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding provided by the National Science Foundation under Grant No. OCE-9806381.</dc:description>

</metadata></record><record><header><identifier>oai:darchive.mblwhoilibrary.org:1912/41</identifier><datestamp>2016-03-08T18:53:10Z</datestamp><setSpec>com_1912_4</setSpec><setSpec>col_1912_7</setSpec><setSpec>col_1912_554</setSpec></header><metadata><oai_dc:dc xmlns:oai_dc="http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc/" xmlns:doc="http://www.lyncode.com/xoai" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xmlns:dc="http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/" xsi:schemaLocation="http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc/ http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc.xsd">
<dc:title>Long–term evolution and coupling of the boundary layers in the Stratus Deck Regions of the eastern Pacific (STRATUS) data report</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Vallee, Charlotte</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Huang, Kelan</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Weller, Robert A.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>STRATUS</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Air-sea interaction</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Data report</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Melville (Ship) Cruise</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Ronald H. Brown (Ship) Cruise RB01-08</dc:subject>
<dc:description>The surface mooring component of the CLIVAR Long Term Evolution and Coupling of the Boundary Layers in the Stratus Deck Regions&#xd;
study (STRATUS) took place from October 2000 in the eastern tropical Pacific. As part of the Eastern Pacific Investigation of Climate&#xd;
Processes in the Coupled Ocean-Atmosphere System (EPIC), STRATUS is a CLIVAR study with the goal of investigating links between sea&#xd;
surface temperature variability in the eastern tropical Pacific and climate over the American continents. This study started a three-year&#xd;
occupation off Chili in order to collect accurate time series of surface forcing and upper ocean variability.&#xd;
The Upper Ocean Processes (UOP) Group at WHOI deployed one fully instrumented surface mooring near 20°S 85°W in October 2000, at the&#xd;
western edge of the stratocumulus cloud deck found west of Peru and Chile, to achieve a good understanding of the role of clouds in the eastern&#xd;
Pacific in modulating atmosphere-ocean coupling. Data from the moorings will improve our understanding of the air-sea fluxes and be used&#xd;
to examine the processes that control sea surface temperature in the cold tongue/intertropical convergence zone (ITCZ) and in the stratus&#xd;
deck region.&#xd;
The first surface mooring (STRATUS 1) was deployed in October 2000 by the UOP group and replaced by a second mooring one year later&#xd;
with almost identical instrumentation (STRATUS 2). STRATUS 1 was equipped with meteorological instrumentation, including two&#xd;
Improved METeorological (IMET) systems. The mooring also carried Vector Measuring Current Meters (VMCMs), single point temperature,&#xd;
salinity and conductivity recorders, and an acoustic Doppler Current Profiler (ADCP) to monitor the upper 500m of the ocean. In addition to&#xd;
the traditional instruments, several other experimental instruments were deployed with limited success on the mooring line including an&#xd;
acoustic current meter, bio-optical instrumentation packages, and an acoustic rain gauge.&#xd;
This report describes the instrumentation deployed on the first STRATUS surface mooring (STRATUS 1 mooring) from October 2000 to&#xd;
October 2001, along with information on the processing and quality control of the returned data. It presents a detailed overview of the&#xd;
meteorological and physical oceanographic data including time series plots, statistics and spectra of key parameters. It also presents the&#xd;
estimated air-sea heat, moisture and momentum fluxes.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration under Grant Numbers NA81RJ1223.</dc:description>

<dc:title>The Northwest Tropical Atlantic Station (NTAS) : NTAS-2 mooring turnaround cruise report</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Plueddemann, Albert J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Ostrom, William M.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Galbraith, Nancy R.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Bouchard, Paul R.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Tupper, George H.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Dunn, James M.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Walsh, M. Alexander</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Air-sea interaction</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Tropical Atlantic</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Moored instrumentation</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Ronald H. Brown (Ship) Cruise RB02-02</dc:subject>
<dc:description>The Northwest Tropical Atlantic Station (NTAS) was established to address the&#xd;
need for accurate air-sea flux estimates and upper ocean measurements in a region with&#xd;
strong sea surface temperature anomalies and the likelihood of significant local air–sea&#xd;
interaction on interannual to decadal timescales. The approach is to maintain a surface&#xd;
mooring outfitted for meteorological and oceanographic measurements at a site near&#xd;
15°N, 51°W by successive mooring turnarounds. These observations will be used to&#xd;
investigate air–sea interaction processes related to climate variability.&#xd;
Deployment of the first NTAS mooring (NTAS-1) at 14°50′ N, 51°00′ W on 30&#xd;
March 2001 was documented in a previous report (Plueddemann et al., 2001). This report&#xd;
documents recovery of the NTAS-1 mooring and deployment of the NTAS-2 mooring at&#xd;
the same site. Both moorings used 3-meter discus buoys as the surface element. These&#xd;
buoys were outfitted with two Air–Sea Interaction Meteorology (ASIMET) systems.&#xd;
Each system measures, records, and transmits via Argos satellite the surface&#xd;
meteorological variables necessary to compute air–sea fluxes of heat, moisture and&#xd;
momentum. The upper 120 m of the NTAS-1 mooring line, and the upper 150 m of the&#xd;
NTAS-2 mooring line, were outfitted with oceanographic sensors for the measurement of&#xd;
temperature and velocity.&#xd;
The mooring turnaround was done on the NOAA Ship Ronald H. Brown, Cruise&#xd;
RB-02-02, by the Upper Ocean Processes Group of the Woods Hole Oceanographic&#xd;
Institution. The cruise took place between 2 and 8 March 2002. A SeaBeam bathymetry&#xd;
survey of the site was done first, followed by deployment of the NTAS-2 mooring on 4&#xd;
March at approximately 14°44.3′ N, 50°56.8′ W in 5043 m of water. A 24-hour&#xd;
intercomparison period followed, after which the NTAS-1 mooring was recovered. This&#xd;
report describes these operations, as well as some of the pre-cruise buoy preparations.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration and the Cooperative Institute for Climate and Ocean Research (CICOR) under Grant No. NA17RJ1223.</dc:description>

<dc:title>North Brazil Current Rings Experiment : RAFOS float data report : November 1998 – June 2000</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Wooding, Christine M.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Richardson, Philip L.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Pacheco, Marguerite A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Glickson, Deborah A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Fratantoni, David M.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Tropical Atlantic circulation</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Mesoscale rings</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>RAFOS floats</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Seward Johnson (Ship) Cruise NBC 1</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Seward Johnson (Ship) Cruise NBC 2</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Seward Johnson (Ship) Cruise NBC 3</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Seward Johnson (Ship) Cruise NBC 4</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Twenty-one RAFOS floats were tracked at depths of 200-1000 meters in and around several North Brazil Current Rings between&#xd;
November 1998 and June 2000. This was part of an experiment to study the role of these current rings in transporting upper level&#xd;
South Atlantic water across the equatorial-tropical gyre boundary into the North Atlantic subtropical gyre. The float trajectories in&#xd;
combination with surface drifters and satellite imagery reveal the sometimes complex life histories of several rings and their fate&#xd;
as they collide with the Lesser Antilles Islands. This report describes the float trajectories, the velocity, temperature, and depth&#xd;
time series, and a preliminary analysis of the float data.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Science Foundation under Grant No. OCE-9729765 and OCE-0136477.</dc:description>
<dc:date>2005-07-18T18:47:39Z</dc:date>
<dc:date>2005-07-18T18:47:39Z</dc:date>

<dc:title>Long-term evolution of the coupled boundary layers (Stratus) mooring recovery and deployment cruise report R/V Melville</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Hutto, Lara</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Weller, Robert A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Lord, Jeffrey</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Ryder, James R.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Stuart-Menteth, Alice</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Galbraith, Nancy R.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Bouchard, Paul R.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Maturana, Jenny</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Pizarro, Oscar</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Letelier, Jaime</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>STRATUS</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Air-sea interaction</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Moored data</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Melville (Ship) Cruise Vanc03</dc:subject>
<dc:description>The Long Term Evolution and Coupling of the Boundary Layers Study (referred to as the Stratus&#xd;
Project) is an effort to obtain a reliable multi-year dataset of meteorological and subsurface&#xd;
measurements beneath the stratus cloud deck off the coast of Chile and Peru. This data will&#xd;
improve our understanding of the role of clouds in ocean-atmosphere coupling. This project is&#xd;
part of the Eastern Pacific Investigation of Climate (EPIC), a NOAA-funded Climate Variability&#xd;
(CLIVAR) study.&#xd;
During the Stratus 2002 cruise, a surface mooring that had been deployed for one year off the&#xd;
coast of Chile was recovered, and a new surface mooring was deployed in the same location.&#xd;
The 2002 deployment starts the final year of a three-year occupation of the site by a Woods Hole&#xd;
Oceanographic Institution (WHOI) mooring as part of the Enhanced Monitoring element of&#xd;
EPIC. The occupation of the site will be continued under the NOAA Climate Observations&#xd;
Program, with the mooring serving as a Surface Reference Site.&#xd;
The Stratus buoys were equipped with surface meteorological instrumentation, mainly two&#xd;
Improved METeorological (IMET) systems. The moorings also carried subsurface equipment&#xd;
attached to the mooring line, which measured conductivity, temperature, current direction and&#xd;
velocity, chlorophyll-a, and rainfall.&#xd;
The moorings were recovered and deployed by the Upper Ocean Processes Group of WHOI&#xd;
from the Scripps Institution of Oceanography’s R/V Melville. In collaboration with investigators&#xd;
from the Chilean Navy Hydrographic and Oceanographic Service (SHOA) and the University of&#xd;
Concepcion, Chile, conductivity, temperature, and depth (CTD) profiles were obtained at the&#xd;
mooring site and along 20°S while steaming east from the mooring site.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration&#xd;
under Grant Number NA17RJ1223.</dc:description>

<dc:title>KAPEX RAFOS float data report 1997-1999 part B : float trajectories at 750 m in the Benguela Current</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Richardson, Philip L.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Pacheco, M. A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Wooding, Christine M.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Southeastern Atlantic circulation</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Mesoscale rings</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>RAFOS floats</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Seward Johnson (Ship) Cruise SJ97-05</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Thirty-two RAFOS floats were launched at the depth of intermediate water, near&#xd;
750 m, in the Benguela Current along 30S and its extension along 7W. The floats were tracked&#xd;
acoustically for two years during 1997–1999. Seven floats looped in three Agulhas Current&#xd;
rings, which drifted west northwestward at a mean velocity of around 5 cm/sec. Floats not in&#xd;
Agulhas rings tended to drift westward at around 2 cm/sec in the latitude band 22S–35S. North&#xd;
of 22S three floats drifted eastward. This report describes the float trajectories and summarizes&#xd;
the main results. These are the first subsurface long-term Lagrangian data in the Benguela&#xd;
Current.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Science Foundation under Grant Nos. OCE-9528574 and OCE-0236654.</dc:description>

<dc:title>The Northwest Tropical Atlantic Station (NTAS) :  NTAS-3 mooring turnaround cruise report</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Plueddemann, Albert J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Ostrom, William M.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Galbraith, Nancy R.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Smith, Jason C.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Ryder, James R.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Holley, Jason J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Walsh, M. Alexander</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Air-sea interaction</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Tropical Atlantic</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Moored instrumentation</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Oceanus (Ship : 1975-) Cruise OC385-5</dc:subject>
<dc:description>The Northwest Tropical Atlantic Station (NTAS) was established to address the&#xd;
need for accurate air-sea flux estimates and upper ocean measurements in a region with&#xd;
strong sea surface temperature anomalies and the likelihood of significant local air–sea&#xd;
interaction on interannual to decadal timescales. The approach is to maintain a surface&#xd;
mooring outfitted for meteorological and oceanographic measurements at a site near&#xd;
15°N, 51°W by successive mooring turnarounds. These observations will be used to&#xd;
investigate air–sea interaction processes related to climate variability.&#xd;
Deployment of the first (NTAS-1) and second (NTAS-2) moorings were&#xd;
documented in previous reports (Plueddemann et al., 2001, 2002). This report documents&#xd;
recovery of the NTAS-2 mooring and deployment of the NTAS-3 mooring at the same&#xd;
site. Both moorings used 3-meter discus buoys as the surface element. These buoys were&#xd;
outfitted with two Air–Sea Interaction Meteorology (ASIMET) systems. Each system&#xd;
measures, records, and transmits via Argos satellite the surface meteorological variables&#xd;
necessary to compute air–sea fluxes of heat, moisture and momentum. The upper 150 m&#xd;
of the mooring line were outfitted with oceanographic sensors for the measurement of&#xd;
temperature and velocity.&#xd;
The mooring turnaround was done on the WHOI R/V Oceanus, Cruise OC-385-5,&#xd;
by the Upper Ocean Processes Group of the Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution. The&#xd;
cruise took place between 12 and 23 February 2003. Deployment of the NTAS-3 mooring&#xd;
was on 15 February at approximately 14°49.5¢ N, 51°01.3¢ W in 4977 m of water. A 24-&#xd;
hour intercomparison period followed, after which the NTAS-2 mooring was recovered.&#xd;
This report describes these operations, as well as some of the pre-cruise buoy&#xd;
preparations.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration&#xd;
under Grant Number NA17RJ1223.</dc:description>

<dc:title>SecNav / CBLAST 2002 field experiment deployment / recovery cruises and data report, F/V Nobska, June 19-20, 2002, F/V Nobska, September 4 and 9, 2002, mooring data, June 19 - September 9, 2002</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Hutto, Lara</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Lord, Jeffrey</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Bouchard, Paul R.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Weller, Robert A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Pritchard, Mark</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>CBLAST-LOW</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Air-sea interaction</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Low wind</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Nobska (Ship) Cruise</dc:subject>
<dc:description>During the summer of 2002, six surface moorings  and one subsurface mooring were deployed south of Martha's Vineyard, Cape Cod, Massachusetts. The moorings were deployed from June to September 2002 to collect meteorological and oceanographic data. This was done both to support the Coupled Boundary Layered Air-Sea Transfer Low wind (CBLAST-Low) cooperative experiment and to address the question of regional predictability in the littoral regime under research supported by a Secretary of the Navy/Chief of Naval Operations (CNO) Chair. The aim was to capture the mesoscale development and response of inner shelf waters to local synoptic atmospheric, tidal and larger scale oceanic forcing under predominantly low wind conditions.  &#xd;
This report covers the operational aspects of the 2002 experiment, including deployment, recovery, and mooring setups, as well as basic data returns.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the Office of Naval Research&#xd;
under Contract Numbers N00014-01-1-0029 and N00014-99-1-0090.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Beaufort Gyre Freshwater Experiment : deployment operations and technology 2003</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Ostrom, William M.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Kemp, John N.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Krishfield, Richard A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Proshutinsky, Andrey</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Arctic operations</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Mooring deployments</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Beaufort Gyre Freshwater Experiment</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Louis S. St. Laurent (Ship) Cruise JWACS</dc:subject>
<dc:description>The Beaufort Gyre Freshwater Experiment (BGFE) observational program was designed to&#xd;
measure the freshwater content of the upper ocean and sea ice in the Beaufort Gyre of the Arctic&#xd;
Ocean using bottom-tethered moorings, drifting buoys, and hydrographic stations. The mooring&#xd;
program required the development of a safe and efficient deployment method by which the&#xd;
subsurface system could be deployed in waters surrounded by sea ice. This report documents the&#xd;
mooring procedure used to deploy the three BGFE moorings from the CCGS Louis S. St-&#xd;
Laurent, during the Joint Western Arctic Climate Study – 2003 (August 6 – September 7). The&#xd;
technical details of the instrumentation attached to each mooring and the specific deployment&#xd;
parameters are described. Specifics pertaining to the deployment of four surface-tethered drifters&#xd;
in the ice are also documented.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Science Foundation under Grant Number OPP-0230184.</dc:description>

<dc:title>A high-resolution bathymetry map for the Marguerite Bay and adjacent west Antarctic Peninsula shelf for the Southern Ocean GLOBEC Program</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Bolmer, S. Thompson</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Beardsley, Robert C.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Pudsey, C.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Morris, P.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Wiebe, Peter H.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Hofmann, Eileen E.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Anderson, J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Maldonado, A.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Bathymetry</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Marguerite Bay</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>SO GLOBEC</dc:subject>
<dc:description>One objective of the U.S. Southern Ocean Global Ocean Ecosystems&#xd;
Dynamics (SO GLOBEC) program is to gain a better understanding of the&#xd;
sea floor bathymetry in the program study area. Much of Marguerite Bay&#xd;
and the adjacent shelf west of the Antarctic Peninsula were poorly charted&#xd;
when the SO GLOBEC program started in 2000. Before the first SO&#xd;
GLOBEC cruise, an improved local area version (ETOPO8.2A) was created&#xd;
from the Smith and Sandwell (1997) topo_8.2.img 2-minute digital gridded&#xd;
bathymetry for the study area. The first SO GLOBEC mooring cruise on&#xd;
the R/V Lawrence M. Gould (March 2001) showed that the 2-minute spatial&#xd;
resolution of ETOPO8.2A did not resolve many of the canyons and abrupt&#xd;
changes in topography that characterize Marguerite Bay and the inner- to&#xd;
mid-shelf region. It also was not particularly accurate in the more uniform&#xd;
terrain regions. We then decided to collect as much multibeam bathymetry&#xd;
data as possible during the SO GLOBEC broad-scale survey cruises on the&#xd;
R/VIB Nathaniel B. Palmer and combine these data with all other&#xd;
available multibeam and trackline bathymetry data to construct a digital&#xd;
bathymetry database and map for the study area. The resulting database&#xd;
has high-resolution data over much of the shelf and parts of Marguerite&#xd;
Bay gridded at 2 seconds in latitude and 6 seconds in longitude spacing&#xd;
between 65° to 71° S and 65° to 78° W. This technical report describes the&#xd;
steps taken to assemble and construct this database and how to access the&#xd;
data via the Internet.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the Office of Naval Research under Contract No. N00014-99-1-0213.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Stratus Ocean Reference Station (20˚S, 85˚W), mooring recovery and deployment cruise R/V Revelle cruise dana 03, November 10 - November 26, 2003</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Hutto, Lara</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Weller, Robert A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Lord, Jeffrey</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Smith, Jason C.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Ryder, James R.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Galbraith, Nancy R.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Fairall, Christopher W.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Stalin, Scott</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Andueza, Juan Carlos</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Tomlinson, Jason</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Air-sea interaction</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Stratus clouds</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Climate prediction</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Roger Revelle (Ship) Cruise Dana 3</dc:subject>
<dc:description>The Ocean Reference Station at 20°S, 85°W under the stratus clouds west of northern&#xd;
Chile and Peru is being maintained to provide ongoing, climate-quality records of surface&#xd;
meteorology, of air-sea fluxes of heat, freshwater, and momentum, and of upper ocean&#xd;
temperature, salinity, and velocity variability. The Stratus Ocean Reference Station,&#xd;
hereafter ORS Stratus, is supported by the National Oceanic and Atmospheric&#xd;
Administrations (NOAA) Climate Observation Program. It is recovered and redeployed&#xd;
annually, with cruises that have come in October or November.&#xd;
During the November 2003 cruise of Scripps Institution of Oceanography's R/V Roger&#xd;
Revelle to the ORS Stratus site, the primary activities where the recovery of the WHOI&#xd;
surface mooring that had been deployed in October 2002, the deployment of a new WHOI&#xd;
surface mooring at that site, the in-situ calibration of the buoy meteorological sensors by&#xd;
comparison with instrumentation put on board by Chris Fairall of the NOAA&#xd;
Environmental Technology Laboratory (ETL), and observations of the stratus clouds and&#xd;
lower atmosphere by NOAA ETL and Jason Tomlinson from Texas A&amp;M.&#xd;
The ORS Stratus buoys are equipped with two Improved Meteorological systems, which&#xd;
provide surface wind speed and direction, air temperature, relative humidity, barometric&#xd;
pressure, incoming shortwave radiation, incoming longwave radiation, precipitation rate,&#xd;
and sea surface temperature. The IMET data are made available in near real time using&#xd;
satellite telemetry. The mooring line carries instruments to measure ocean salinity,&#xd;
temperature, and currents. On some deployments, additional instrumentation is attached to&#xd;
the mooring to measure rainfall and bio-optical variability. The ETL instrumentation used&#xd;
during the 2003 cruise included a cloud radar, radiosonde balloons, and sensors for mean&#xd;
and turbulent surface meteorology.&#xd;
In addition to this work, buoy work was done in support of the Ecuadorian Navy Institute&#xd;
of Oceanography (INOCAR) and of the Chilean Navy Hydrographic and Oceanographic&#xd;
Service (SHOA). The surface buoy, oceanographic instrumentation, and upper 500 m of&#xd;
an INOCAR surface mooring at 2°S, 84°W that had been vandalized were recovered and&#xd;
transferred to the Ecuadorian Navy vessel B. A. E. Calicuchima. A tsunami warning&#xd;
mooring was installed at 75°W, 20°S for SHOA. SHOA personnel onboard were trained&#xd;
during the cruise by staff from the NOAA Pacific Marine Environmental Laboratory&#xd;
(PMEL) and National Data Buoy Center (NDBC). The cruise hosted two teachers&#xd;
participating in NOAA's Teacher at Sea Program, Deb Brice from San Marcos, California&#xd;
and Viviana Zamorano from Arica, Chile.</dc:description>

<dc:description>The primary objective of this publication is to share with a wider audience the valuable&#xd;

the second in a series of planned workshops on the science and management of coastal landforms&#xd;

on January 24, 2001. The individuals who attended this workshop are actively engaged in&#xd;
planning, managing, regulating, engineering, educating, and studying coastal landforms and&#xd;
their beneficial functions. This workshop titled, Can Humans &amp; Coastal Landforms Co-exist?’, was&#xd;
a natural follow-up to a previous workshop, Coastal Landform Management in Massachusetts, held&#xd;
at WHOI October 9-10, 1997 (proceedings published as WHOI Technical Report #WHOI-98-16).&#xd;
The workshop had a very practical, applied focus, providing state-of-the-art scientific understanding&#xd;
of coastal landform function, case history management and regulation of human activities&#xd;
proposed on coastal landforms, a multi-faceted mock conservation commission hearing&#xd;
presented by practicing technical consultants and attorneys that involved all attendees acting as&#xd;
regulators in breakout sessions, and, at the conclusion of the workshop, an open discussion on&#xd;
all issues related to the science and management of coastal landforms, including future research&#xd;
needs.</dc:description>
