<dc:description>The primary objective of the workshop and these proceedings is to share with a broader audience&#xd;
the valuable information and extensive dialogue that took place amongst over 100 individuals&#xd;
who attended the third in a series of workshops on the science and management of coastal&#xd;
landforms in Massachusetts. The workshop took place at the Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution&#xd;
(WHOI), Woods Hole, MA on February 28, 2002. The individuals who attended the workshop&#xd;
are actively engaged in planning, managing, regulating, engineering, educating, and studying the&#xd;
interaction of human activities with coastal landforms and coastal processes, particularly erosion&#xd;
control related activities.&#xd;
This workshop titled, Stabilizing Dunes and Coastal Banks using Vegetation and Bioengineering,&#xd;
was a natural follow-up to two previous workshops: Can Humans and Coastal Landforms Co-exist,&#xd;
held at WHOI, January 24, 2001 (proceedings published as WHOI Technical Report #WHOI-2001-14), and Coastal Landform Management in Massachusetts, held at WHOI October 9-10, 1997 (proceedings&#xd;
published as WHOI Technical Report #WHOI-98-16).&#xd;
This workshop had a very practical, applied focus, providing state-of-the-art scientific and case&#xd;
history engineering applications of non-structural/bioengineering and coastal vegetation-related&#xd;
erosion control and wildlife habitat enhancement techniques. The history and theory of bioengineering&#xd;
in coastal areas was discussed as well.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Red Sea Outflow Experiment (REDSOX) : DLD2 RAFOS float data report February 2001 - March 2003</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Furey, Heather H.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Bower, Amy S.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Fratantoni, David M.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Marginal sea overflow</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Float</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Indian Ocean</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Knorr (Ship : 1970-) Cruise KN162-10</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Knorr (Ship : 1970-) Cruise KN162-11</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Maurice Ewing (Ship) Cruise EW110</dc:subject>
<dc:description>This is the final data report of all acoustically tracked second-generation Deep Lagrangian Drifter (DLD2)&#xd;
RAFOS float data collected by the Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution in 2001-2003 during the Red&#xd;
Sea Outflow Experiment (REDSOX). The float component of REDSOX was comprised of two&#xd;
deployments on the R/V Knorr and R/V Ewing: the first in February-March 2001, with 26 floats, and the&#xd;
second in August-September 2001, with 27 floats. The isobaric floats were ballasted for 650 decibars to&#xd;
target the intermediate-depth, high-salinity outflow waters from the Red Sea. The objectives of the&#xd;
Lagrangian float study were (1) to identify the spreading pathways of the equilibrated Red Sea outflow,&#xd;
and to quantify the velocities and eddy variability typical of this outflow and of the background oceanic&#xd;
environment in the Gulf of Aden, and (2) to identify and describe the mesoscale processes which&#xd;
contribute to the seaward transport of Red Sea Overflow Water properties through the Gulf of Aden and&#xd;
into the western Indian Ocean. In addition to floats activated and launched during the two cruises, four&#xd;
time-series sites were chosen for dual-release float moorings. The dual-release floats were released&#xd;
every two months between cruises and every two months after the second cruise, with the final release in&#xd;
March 2002. A pirate attack on the R/V Ewing forced some modification of the float deployment plan&#xd;
during the second cruise.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Science Foundation under Grant Number OCE-9818464.</dc:description>

<dc:title>RAFOS float processing at the Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Wooding, Christine M.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Furey, Heather H.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Pacheco, Marguerite A.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Float</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Processing</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Software</dc:subject>
<dc:description>This report and its accompanying webpage (http://www.whoi.edu/science/PO/rafos/index.htm)&#xd;
describe the processing steps for RAFOS floats, from subscribing to Service Argos to plotting&#xd;
the final data for a data report. The MATLAB-based tracking software ARTOA-II is described&#xd;
in detail. ARTOA-II allows the user to convert raw hexidecimal data to decimal format,&#xd;
calculate float clock drifts, edit raw data, and use the time-of-arrival data to calculate float&#xd;
trajectories. The intention is that this report will provide a handy reference and it will be kept&#xd;
from becoming out-of-date by updates to the relevant webpages.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Elevators - autonomous transporters for deep sea benthic sample recovery</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Bowen, Martin F.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Bernard, P. J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Gleason, D. E.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Whitcomb, Louis L.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>ROV</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Sampling</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Autonomous</dc:subject>
<dc:description>May also be cited as: WHOI-00-01</dc:description>
<dc:description>Two classes of sample recovery devices have been fabricated for use with the Jason deep diving remotely operated vehicle (ROV). One class includes custom-built, autonomous vertical transporters (AVTs) that are capable of raising numerous benthic samples and other payloads to the ocean's surface independent of the sampling RAOV. AVTs are more commonly referred to as sample "elevators". Elevators are inexpensive to construct and can be deployed and recovered many times during the course of a single ROV dive in order to reduce wear and tear on the more expensive ROV. A second class includes a range of custom-built end effectors and articulated scoops that ROV operators mount onto the vehicle's manipulator and sampling cradle according to required sampling tasks. This report describes the design, fabrication, operation, and navigational tracking of these various non-destructive sampling devices. A ballast and flotation spreadsheeet is provided, which allows operators to customize each elevator deployment and sample recovery scenario while the ROV continues its work on the benthos.</dc:description>
<dc:date>2005-07-19T18:18:19Z</dc:date>

</metadata></record><record><header><identifier>oai:darchive.mblwhoilibrary.org:1912/57</identifier><datestamp>2016-03-08T16:42:10Z</datestamp><setSpec>com_1912_4</setSpec><setSpec>col_1912_8</setSpec><setSpec>col_1912_554</setSpec></header><metadata><oai_dc:dc xmlns:oai_dc="http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc/" xmlns:doc="http://www.lyncode.com/xoai" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xmlns:dc="http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/" xsi:schemaLocation="http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc/ http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc.xsd">
<dc:title>Tidal observations at Ria Formosa, Algarve, Portugal</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Salles, Paulo</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>O'Malley, Stephen P.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Voulgaris, George</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Aubrey, David G.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Tidal inlets</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Tidal distortion</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Numerical modeling</dc:subject>
<dc:description>The apparent persistence and stability of multiple tidal inlets in coastal lagoons are important for a variety of reasons, such as water quality, navigability and beach/barrier stability. To identify and study the processes controlling the persistence of multiple tidal inlets, the hydrodynamics of the system have to be better understood.&#xd;
This project is part of a larger study (INDIA) examining general tidal inlet processes. The present components consist of a numerical simulation study of processes controlling multiple inlet stability, combined with exhaustive field measurements. This report addresses only the second component. The analysis uses as study site and main source of data the Ria Formosa lagoon in Portugal, which has multiple and historically persistent inlets.&#xd;
For the numerical simulation model, field measurements are needed to provide (i) updated bathymetry of the inlets, in situ measurements of (ii) water level fluctuations within the estuary and (iii) flow velocities through the inlets.&#xd;
This report gives first a brief description of the instrumentation used in the field (section 2), then describes the methods used to deploy the instruments, perform the surveys and gather the data (section 3), explains the procedures for data reduction and show some results (section 4 and Annex).</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by WHOI Sea Grant, EU MAST III Program, UNAM, Mellon Foundation and Rinehart Coastal Research Center.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Knorr 147 leg v hydrographic data report : Labrador Sea Deep Convection Experiment</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Zimmermann, Sarah</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>McKee, Theresa K.</dc:creator>

<dc:subject>Deep convection</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Wintertime circulation</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Knorr (Ship : 1970-) Cruise KN147-5</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Between 2 February and 20 March 1997, the first phase of the Labrador Sea Deep Convection Experiment was carried out on R/V Knorr, during which 127 hydrographic stations were occupied throughout the Labrador basin. This included five boundary crossings (two on the east and three on the west). Special emphasis was placed on the western portion of the basin were deep convection occurs. Expendable Bathy Thermographs (XBTs) were launched regularly to increase resolution near the boundary and to help optimally place interior stations. Three "to-yo" CTD surveys were conducted, and Langrangian floats were delpoyed throughout the cruise. Despite extremely difficult working conditions, this cruise was successful in observing deep convection under "classic" wintertime conditions. This report describes the CTD operation and performance and also presents vertical profiles of CTD Potential Temperature, Salinity, and Potential Density (referenced to the surface and 1500 db) plotted versus Depth. Instructions for obtaining the data via anonymous FTP are included in Appendix B.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the Office of Naval Research under Contract No. N00014-97-1-0043.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Premilinary results of the effects of SURTASS-LFA sonar on singing humpback whales</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Biassoni, Nicoletta</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Miller, Patrick J. O.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Tyack, Peter L.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Humpback whales</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Disturbance</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Sonar</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Cory Chouest (Ship) Cruise</dc:subject>
<dc:description>The singing behavior of humpback whales exposed to SURTASS-LFA sonar was monitored in Hawaii during March 1998. An observation vessel towing a hydrophone array followed individual singers continuously recording their songs and the received level of sonar near the whale. At least 2 complete songs were recorded before commencing a 60min 42s long playback, which consisted of ten 42s signals transmitted every 6 min. by a U.S. Navy vessel. Observations continued into the post-exposure period. Song spectrograms were broken into themes and phrases using visual analysis and aurol scoring. 23 focal follows were conducted; 5 were control follows with no playback. In 9 follows the whale sang throughout the playback, in 4 the whale stopped singing when it joined another whale and in 5 it stopped presumably in response to the playback. We recorded at least one complete song in all three experimental conditions from six individuals. These singers sang longer sonsgs during (13.75min) than before (10.68min) or after (10.58min) the playback (model III ANOVA, p=0.047, n=6). No differences were found in theme order. Song cessation and song duration responses did not scale with sonar received level. High variability in individual responses may indicate that some males were more sensitive to the sonar than others.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the Office of Naval Research under Contract No. N00014-97-1-1031.</dc:description>

<dc:title>A comparison of buoy meteorological systems</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Payne, Richard E.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Huang, Kelan</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Weller, Robert A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Freitag, H. P.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Cronin, Meghan F.</dc:creator>

<dc:creator>Meinig, C.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Kuroda, Yoshifumi</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Ushijima, Norifumi</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Reynolds, R. Michael</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Meteorological sensor intercomparison</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Meteorological sensor performance</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Moored instrument measurements</dc:subject>
<dc:description>During May and June 2000, an intercomparison was made of buoy meteorological&#xd;
systems from the Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution (WHOI), the National Oceanographic&#xd;
and Atmospheric Administration (NOAA), Pacific Marine Environmental Laboratory (PMEL),&#xd;
and the Japanese Marine Science and Technology Center (JAMSTEC). Two WHOI systems&#xd;
mounted on a 3 m discus buoy, two PMEL systems mounted on separate buoy tower tops and&#xd;
one JAMSTEC system mounted on a wooden platform were lined parallel to, and 25 m from&#xd;
Nantucket Sound in Massachusetts. All systems used R. M. Young propeller anemometers,&#xd;
Rotronic relative humidity and air temperature sensors and Eppley short-wave radiation sensors.&#xd;
The PMEL and WHOI systems used R. M.Young self-siphoning rain gauges, while the&#xd;
JAMSTEC system used a Scientific Technology ORG-115 optical rain gauge. The PMEL and&#xd;
WHOI systems included an Eppley PIR long-wave sensor, while the JAMSTEC had no longwave&#xd;
sensor. The WHOI system used an AIR DB-1A barometric pressure sensor. PMEL and&#xd;
JAMSTEC systems used Paroscientific Digiquartz sensors. The Geophysical Instruments and&#xd;
Measurements Group (GIM) from Brookhaven National Laboratory (BNL) installed two&#xd;
Portable Radiation Package (PRP) systems that include Eppley short-wave and long-wave&#xd;
sensors on a platform near the site.&#xd;
It was apparent from the data that for most of the sensors, the correlation between data&#xd;
sets was better than the absolute agreement between them. The conclusions made were that the&#xd;
sensors and associated electronics from the three different laboratories performed comparably.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration&#xd;
under Grant Number NA96GPO429.</dc:description>

<dc:title>WHOI silhouette DIGITIZER version 1.0  user’s guide</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Little, William S.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Copley, Nancy J.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Zooplankton length</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Biomass estimate</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Software</dc:subject>
<dc:description>WHOI Silhouette DIGITIZER is a MATLAB-based computer program for measuring the lengths of&#xd;
marine organisms in the macrozooplankton size range. DIGITIZER displays a scanned&#xd;
photographic image of a seawater slurry containing large numbers of marine organisms, upon&#xd;
which is superimposed a reference grid. DIGITIZER then allows you to measure the organisms'&#xd;
lengths using the cursor on the computer screen. DIGITIZER automatically calculates each&#xd;
organism’s biomass and generates spreadsheet compatible output listings of basic statistics&#xd;
derived from the data. DIGITIZER also produces text files of lengths, weights, and size-frequency&#xd;
histograms.</dc:description>

under Grant Nos. OCE-9806381, OCE-9940880, and OCE-0095069.</dc:description>

<dc:title>WHOI Cable v2.0 : time domain numerical simulation of moored and towed oceanographic systems</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Gobat, Jason I.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Grosenbaugh, Mark A.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Mooring dynamics</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Cable dynamics</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Towed systems</dc:subject>
<dc:description>This report describes version 2.0 of a numerical program for analyzing the statics and dynamics of cable structures commonly encountered in oceanographic engineering practice. The numerical program, WHOI Cable, features a nonlinear solver that includes the effects of geometric and material nonlinearities, bending stiffness for seamless modeling of slack cables, and a model for the interaction of cable segments with the seafloor. The program solves both surface and subsurface single- and multi-point mooring problems, systems with both ends anchored on the bottom, and towing and drifter problems. Forcing includes waves, current, wind, ship speed, and pay-out of cable. The programs that make-up WHOI Cable run under Unix and Windows. There is familiar  graphical interface available for Windows platforms. The report includes detailed instructions for formulating problem input files and running the programs.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the Office of Naval Research under Contract Nos. N00014-92-J-1269 and N00014-95-1-0106.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Coastal Landform System Sustainability  Project : an analysis of activities permitted on coastal landforms on Cape Cod, Massachusetts in 1999</dc:title>
<dc:creator>O'Connell, James F.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Coastal landform</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Coastal management</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Permits</dc:subject>
<dc:description>In their natural state, the coastal landform systems of Cape Cod are self-sustaining. However, recognition that humans have become intrinsic agents in the evolution of coastal landscapes is significant. There is a great need to understand how individual actions on a small scale (lot-by-lot) basis affect the sustainability of coastal landform systems, such as coastal dunes, beaches, coastal banks, barrier beaches, saltmarshes, and coastal floodplains. However, there are few investigations relative to this scale. &#xd;
This study illustrates the vast extent of human alterations to coastal landforms on Cape Cod. As a result of analyzing 318 Orders of Conditions issued for activities permitted on and adjacent to coastal landforms in all 15 Cape Cod towns in 1999, it documents and quantifies the gains and losses to coastal landform system sustainability.&#xd;
The study documents the types of activities presently taking place on and adjacent to our coastal landforms and their potential affects, and potential mitigation being required by local commissions to minimize these affects. It also documents the trade-offs and balances oftentimes necessary in the application of performance standard based regulations governing activities proposed on coastal landforms. Because our quantitative understanding of coastal landform function is still evolving, particularly on a small-scale lot-by-lot basis, many decisions are oftentimes made using best professional judgement (if available) without predictive capability to know what the impact will be to the applicant's or neighboring property and resources.&#xd;
It is hoped that the results of this study will assist local, state, and federal coastal  resource managers and regulators, as well as the public, in gaining insight into the interactions of human activities and natural coastal landform system function leading towards improved coastal resource management. The project participants stated that during the course of this study the sharing of information among them was invaluable. It is hoped that the sharing of information in this study with a broader audience will also be utilized for improved coastal landform system management.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Distribution of calling blue, fin, and humpback whales in the North Pacific</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Watkins, William A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Daher, Mary Ann</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>George, Joseph E.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Haga, Scott H.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>North Pacific whales</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Calling whales</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Balaenoptera musculus</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Megaptera novaeangliae</dc:subject>
<dc:description>May also be cited as: WHOI-00-12</dc:description>
<dc:description>The seasonal distribution of calling ble whales (Balaenoptera musculus), fin whales (B. physalus), and singing humpback whales (Megaptera novaeangliae) has been analyzed from acoustic data recorded by the U.S. Navy Sound Surveillance System (SOSUS) and other hydrophone arrays in the North Pacific. The data from ten arrays were selected as representative of four Regions along the continental margins, labeled Northwest (NW), NorthCentral (NC), Northeast (NE), and Southeast (SE). The call data indicate that there is a substantial population of blue whales scattered over the North Pacific and concentrated during the Fall season in the NW and NC Regions. Blue whale calls are recorded in all seasons, particularly in these NW and NC Regions. Fin whales are found in groups but also in relatively large numbers in all Regions, with most calling in the Winter season and in the NC Region, while calling is absent in most Regions during Summer. Fin whale calling includes "F" calls from individuals heard in all seasons and "J" calls from too many whales to separate, particularly prominent in the winter reproductive season. Humpback singing is recorded most in the both the NC and SE Regions between the 1996-1997 and 1998-2000 periods.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the Office of Naval Research under Contract No. N00014-96-1-1130 CNO-N45 Environmental Program and U.S. Army Corps of Engineers (DCA87-00/H-0026) with funds from the Department of Defense Legacy Reseource Management Program.</dc:description>
<dc:date>2005-07-20T20:28:51Z</dc:date>

</metadata></record><record><header><identifier>oai:darchive.mblwhoilibrary.org:1912/66</identifier><datestamp>2016-03-08T18:58:55Z</datestamp><setSpec>com_1912_4</setSpec><setSpec>col_1912_7</setSpec><setSpec>col_1912_554</setSpec></header><metadata><oai_dc:dc xmlns:oai_dc="http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc/" xmlns:doc="http://www.lyncode.com/xoai" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xmlns:dc="http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/" xsi:schemaLocation="http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc/ http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc.xsd">
<dc:title>An improved long-wave radiometer</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Anderson, Steven P.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Payne, Richard E.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Long-wave radiometer</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Meteorological sensor</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Thermistors</dc:subject>
<dc:description>May also be cited as: WHOI-00-13</dc:description>
<dc:description>This report describes the development of an improved long-wave radiometer (pyrgeometer) for deployment on ships and buoys. Standard pyrgeometers use a thermopile to measure the temperature gradient between the receiver surface and the instrument case, and thus infer the receiver temperature and incident radiation. The key design change employed in the new radiometer is to remove the thermopile and replace it with a small, glass-encapsulated thermistor to measure the receiver temperature directly. To prove the concept, a prototype radiometer was built and calibrated. It was then deployed outside for a period of a week on the roof of the Clark Laboratory (Quissett Campus, Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution) to demonstrate the feasability of the new concept. Data from the prototype were compared to those from a pair of standard radiometers. The intercomparison shows that the prototype performed surprisingly well. It was able to capture all the variability observed by the standards with only a small bias. The next step in the design process, which has been funded by the National Science Foundation, is to build a rugged version of the prototype that can be deployed in the field.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the Cecil H. and Ida M. Green Technology Innovations Awards Program and the National Science Foundation, Grant OCE98-18470.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Thermoconvective eddies in air : application to meteorology</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Avsec, Dusan</dc:creator>
<dc:contributor>Ronne, Carl A.</dc:contributor>
<dc:subject>Vortex-motion</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Heat</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Convection</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Eddies</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Translated from the French by Carl A. Ronne.</dc:description>
<dc:description>The object of the present work is a study thermoconvective eddies in a layer of air heated uniformly from below. Being given the importance that these eddies present in meteorlogical phenomena, we have tried to attain three principal objectives: 1. To realize systematic experiments on thermoconvective eddies in air under thicknesses exceeding considerably the scale generally employed by preceding experimenters; 2. To verify experimentally the practical value of the numerical results deriving from the theory of thermoconvective eddies; 3. To complete, by the results acquired in the course of our researches in the laboratory, the theory of thermoconvective eddies in the free atmosphere.</dc:description>
<dc:date>2005-07-21T13:15:22Z</dc:date>
<dc:date>2005-07-21T13:15:22Z</dc:date>
<dc:date>1939</dc:date>
<dc:type>Technical Report</dc:type>
<dc:identifier>http://hdl.handle.net/1912/67</dc:identifier>

<dc:title>Dissolved oxygen measurments in sea water at the Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Knapp, George P.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Stalcup, Marvel C.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Stanley, Robert J.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Dissolved oxygen</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Winkler titration</dc:subject>
<dc:description>This report describes a modified Winkler titration technique that has been used for the past 25 years at the Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution (WHOI). During this time most of the dissolved oxygen measurements made at sea by WHOI personnel have been analyzed with this technique and only relatively minor, evolutionary changes in the procedures and equipment have occurred. These changes, however, have improved the precision and accuracy of deep-sea dissolved oxygen measurements to 0.005 ml/l and 0.02 ml/l respectively.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Science Foundation through grant Number OCE 87-16910.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Automated oxygen titration and salinity determination</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Knapp, George P.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Stalcup, Marvel C.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Stanley, Robert J.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Dissolved oxygen analysis</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Automated salinity analysis</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Hydrography</dc:subject>
<dc:description>This report describes a newly developed automated Winkler titration system for dissolved oxygen in seawater which is presently in use at the Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution. This amperometric, calculated, endpoint system was compared with two different automated and one manual Winkler method during a recent cruise. The four different methods agreed to within about  0.04 ml/l. The system described here measures the titrant needed to reach the endpoint with a resolution better than 0.001 ml. The standard deviation of replicate samples is 0.005 ml/l and the accuracy is about 0.02 ml/l. A technique to automatically acquire conductivity ration measurements and calculate salinity using a Guildline Autosal Salinometer is also described.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Science Foundation through Grant No. OCE88-22542.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Estimating the economic benefits of regional ocean observing systems</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Kite-Powell, Hauke L.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Colgan, Charles S.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Wellman, Katharine F.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Pelsoci, Thomas</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Wieand, Kenneth</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Pendleton, Linwood</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Kaiser, Mark J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Pulsipher, Allan G.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Luger, Michael</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Ocean observing system</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Economic benefits</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Coastal ocean</dc:subject>
<dc:description>We develop a methodology to estimate the potential economic benefits from new investments in&#xd;
regional coastal ocean observing systems in US waters, and apply this methodology to generate&#xd;
preliminary estimates of such benefits. The approach focuses on potential economic benefits&#xd;
from coastal ocean observing information within ten geographic regions encompassing all coastal&#xd;
waters of the United States, and within a wide range of industrial and recreational activities&#xd;
including recreational fishing and boating, beach recreation, maritime transportation, search and&#xd;
rescue operations, spill response, marine hazards prediction, offshore energy, power generation,&#xd;
and commercial fishing.&#xd;
Our findings suggest that annual benefits to users from the deployment of ocean observing&#xd;
systems are likely to run in the multiple $100s of millions of dollars per year.&#xd;
The project results should be considered first-order estimates that are subject to considerable&#xd;
refinement as the parameters of regional observing systems are better defined, and as our&#xd;
understanding of user sectors improves.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the Office of Naval Research under Grant No. N00014-02-1-1037.</dc:description>

<dc:title>CBLAST 2003 field work report</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Hutto, Lara</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Farrar, J. Thomas</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Weller, Robert A.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Air-sea interaction</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Upper ocean dynamics</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Mixed layer</dc:subject>
<dc:description>The long-range scientific objective of the Coupled Boundary Layer Air Sea Transfer&#xd;
(CBLAST) project is to observe and understand the temporal and spatial variability of the&#xd;
upper ocean, to identify the processes that determine that variability, and to examine its&#xd;
predictability. Air-sea interaction is of particular interest, but attention is also paid to the&#xd;
coupling of the sub-thermocline ocean to the mixed layer and to both the open ocean and&#xd;
littoral regimes. We seek to do this over a wide range of environmental conditions with&#xd;
the intent of improving our understanding of upper ocean dynamics and of the physical&#xd;
processes that determine the vertical and horizontal structure of the upper ocean.&#xd;
Field work for CBLAST was conducted during the summers of 2001, 2002, and 2003 off&#xd;
the south shore of Martha’s Vineyard, Massachusetts. The 2003 field work was conducted&#xd;
from the following platforms: heavy moorings, light moorings, drifters, F/V Nobska,&#xd;
CIRPAS Pelican aircraft, and an IR Cessna Aircraft. This report documents the 2003 field&#xd;
work and includes field notes, platform descriptions, discussion of data returns, and&#xd;
mooring logs. The 2003 Intensive Operating Period (IOP) was very successful and a high&#xd;
data return was seen.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the Office of Naval Research under contract numbers&#xd;
N00014-01-1-0029 and N00014-05-10090.</dc:description>

<dc:title>The Beaufort Gyre Observing System 2004 : mooring recovery and deployment operations in pack ice</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Kemp, John N.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Newhall, Kris</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Ostrom, William M.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Krishfield, Richard A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Proshutinsky, Andrey</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Arctic operations</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Mooring deployments</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Beaufort Gyre Observing System</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Louis S. St. Laurent (Ship) Cruise JWACS</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Situated beneath the Arctic perennial ice pack, the principal components of the&#xd;
Beaufort Gyre Observing System are three deep-ocean bottom-tethered moorings with&#xd;
CTD and velocity profilers, upward looking sonars for ice draft measurements, and&#xd;
bottom pressure recorders. A major goal of this project is to investigate basin-scale&#xd;
mechanisms regulating freshwater and heat content in the Arctic Ocean and particularly&#xd;
in the Beaufort Gyre throughout several complete annual cycles. The methods of&#xd;
recovering and re-deploying the 3800 m long instrumented moorings from the Canadian&#xd;
Coast Guard Icebreaker Louis S. St. Laurent in August 2004 are described.&#xd;
In ice-covered regions, deployments must be conducted anchor-first, so heavier&#xd;
wire rope and hardware must be incorporated into the mooring design. Backup buoyancy&#xd;
at the bottom of the mooring is advised for backup recovery should intermediate lengths&#xd;
of the mooring system get tangled under ice floes during recovery. An accurate acoustic&#xd;
survey to determine the exact location of the mooring, adequate ice conditions, and&#xd;
skilled ship maneuvering are all essential requirements for a successful mooring&#xd;
recovery. Windlass (or capstan) procedures could be used for the recovery, but a traction&#xd;
winch arrangement is recommended.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Science Foundation under Grant Number OPP-0230184 and&#xd;
Woods Hole Oceanographic Insitution’s Ocean and Climate Change Institute.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Stratus Ocean Reference Station (20˚S, 85˚W), mooring recovery and deployment cruise, R/V Ron Brown cruise 04-11, December 5 - December 24, 2004</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Colbo, Keir</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Weller, Robert A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Lord, Jeffrey</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Smith, Jason C.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Bouchard, Paul R.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Fairall, Christopher W.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Bradley, Frank</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Wolfe, Dan</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Serpetzoglou, Efthymios</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Tomlinson, Jason</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Tisandie, Alvaro Gustave Vera</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Bustos, Juan Francisco Santibanez</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Air-sea fluxes</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Upper ocean variability</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Stratus clouds</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Ronald H. Brown (Ship) Cruise RB04-11</dc:subject>
<dc:description>The Ocean Reference Station at 20° S, 85° W under the stratus clouds west of northern&#xd;
Chile and Peru is being maintained to provide ongoing, climate-quality records of surface&#xd;
meteorology, of air-sea fluxes of heat, freshwater, and momentum, and of upper ocean&#xd;
temperature, salinity, and velocity variability. The Stratus Ocean Reference Station (ORS&#xd;
Stratus) is supported by the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration’s (NOAA)&#xd;
Climate Observation Program. It is recovered and redeployed annually, with cruises that&#xd;
have come between October and December.&#xd;
During the December 2004 cruise of NOAA's R/V Ronald H. Brown to the ORS Stratus&#xd;
site, the primary activities where the recovery of the WHOI surface mooring that had been&#xd;
deployed in November 2003, the deployment of a new WHOI surface mooring at that site,&#xd;
the in-situ calibration of the buoy meteorological sensors by comparison with&#xd;
instrumentation put on board by staff of the NOAA Environmental Technology Laboratory&#xd;
(ETL), and observations of the stratus clouds and lower atmosphere by NOAA ETL and&#xd;
Jason Tomlinson from Texas A&amp;M.&#xd;
The ORS Stratus buoys are equipped with two Improved Meteorological systems, which&#xd;
provide surface wind speed and direction, air temperature, relative humidity, barometric&#xd;
pressure, incoming shortwave radiation, incoming longwave radiation, precipitation rate,&#xd;
and sea surface temperature. The IMET data are made available in near real time using&#xd;
satellite telemetry. The mooring line carries instruments to measure ocean salinity,&#xd;
temperature, and currents. The ETL instrumentation used during the 2004 cruise included&#xd;
cloud radar, radiosonde balloons, and sensors for mean and turbulent surface meteorology.&#xd;
The atmospheric observations also benefited from the C-Band radar mounted on the R/V&#xd;
Ronald H. Brown.&#xd;
In addition to this work, buoy work was done in support of the Chilean Navy&#xd;
Hydrographic and Oceanographic Service (SHOA). A tsunami warning mooring was&#xd;
reinstalled at 75°W, 20°S for SHOA, after the previous buoy installed last year failed.&#xd;
SHOA personnel were onboard to direct the deployment and to gain experience. Four&#xd;
students from the University of Concepcion collected hydrographic data and water&#xd;
samples. One other Chilean student from the University of Chile was involved in the&#xd;
atmospheric sampling program, with a particular focus on the near coast jet. Finally, the&#xd;
cruise hosted a teacher participating in NOAA's Teacher at Sea Program, Mary Esther&#xd;
Cook, who used her experience to develop lessons for her class back in Arkansas.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration under Contract Number NA17RJ1225.</dc:description>

<dc:title>The Belukha whale : natural behavior and bioacoustics</dc:title>
<dc:title>Belukha : povedenie i bioakustika v prirode</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Belkovitch, V.  M.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Shekotov, M. N.</dc:creator>
<dc:contributor>Haney, J. Christopher</dc:contributor>
<dc:contributor>Recchia, Cheri A.</dc:contributor>
<dc:contributor>Svanidze, Marina V.</dc:contributor>
<dc:subject>White whale</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Behavior</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Whale sounds</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Sound production by animals</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Bioacoustics</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Delphinapterus leucas</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Cetacea</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Beluga whale</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Translated from the Russian by Marina A. Svanidze. Edited by J. Christopher Haney and Cheri Recchia.</dc:description>
<dc:description>This monograph presents new original material on the behavior and bioacoustic signals of the belukha whale in its natural environment. A typological classification of the species' signals is based on researches that were conducted for many years. This book as well contains descriptions of the time-frequency characteristics of the main classes of sounds. Comparison of the behavior and signals of the belukha whale synchronized by time, enabled development of an ethological-acoustical model of individual behavioral activity in search and hunting, and this study reveals the function of certain sounds. Also, the study made it possible to obtain data on the navigational mechanism (or orientation mechanism) and emphasizes the role of sounds in all the different beharioral activities of the belukha whale. Studies conducted at the extreme points of its range enabled, for the first time, comparison of the ethological-acoustic attributes of the belukha whale in the White Sea and the Amur River estuary.</dc:description>
<dc:date>2005-08-25T18:28:53Z</dc:date>
<dc:date>2005-08-25T18:28:53Z</dc:date>

<dc:title>Four hydrophone array for acoustic three-dimensional location</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Watkins, William A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Schevill, William E.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Underwater acoustics</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Hydrophone</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Three-dimensional locations</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Array</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Ambient noise</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Instruments</dc:subject>
<dc:description>This report describes a non-rigid, floating, four-hydrophone array that can&#xd;
give accurate three-dimensional locations for certain underwater sound sources,&#xd;
particularly for those that are close. For more distant sounds, the array&#xd;
can indicate good directions (bearings) but range is liable to be uncertain. A&#xd;
system for concurrent calibration permits periodic reassessment of hydrophone positions.&#xd;
Appendix: Programming and mathematics for computer analysis of the array&#xd;
data are by Donna Ekstrand and Mary Hunt.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Supported by the Office of Naval Research&#xd;
under Contract N00014-66-C0241; NR 083-004.</dc:description>
<dc:date>2005-10-19T15:07:18Z</dc:date>

<dc:title>Astrophysical and geophysical flows as dynamical systems : 1998 summer study program in Geophysical Fluid Dynamics</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Balmforth, Neil J.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Nonlinear dynamics</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Fluids</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Fluid models</dc:subject>
<dc:description>The theme of the 1998 Geophyscial Fluid Dynamics (GFD) summer program at the Woods Hole  Oceanographic Institution was Astrophysical and Geophysical Flows as Dynamical Systems. Antonello Provenzale of the Institute of Cosmogeophysics in Italy was  the principal lecturer for the summer, and Charles Tresser of IBM gave a series of seminars on introductory dynamical systems theory. In addition to the usual intense schedule of seminars on a variety of unfocussed topics falling into the general theme of the summer, the program included three "theme weeks." The first was focused on  climate dynamics, the second on bifurcation and pattern theory, and the third was the special "Mixing Week." Ten GFD Fellows, at various stages of their graduate work, undertook supervised research projects. This volume contains notes from the principal lectures and the Fellows' Reports.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the Office of Naval Research under Contract No. N00014-97-1-0934 and by the National Science Foundation under Grant No. OCE-9314484.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Hydrographic data from the U. S. Naval Oceanographic Office : Persian Gulf, Southern Red Sea, and Arabian Sea, 1923-1996</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Alessi, Carol A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Hunt, Heather D.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Bower, Amy S.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Hydrological stations</dc:subject>

<dc:subject>Hydrographic data</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Temperature-salinity-depth profile data were obtained for the Persian Gulf, Southern Red Sea and parts of the Arabian Sea from the Master Oceanographic Data Set (MOODS), located at the U. S. Naval Oceanographic Office (NAVOCEANO), Stennis Space Center, Mississippi.  These data were used as part of a physical oceanographic study of the Red Sea  and Persian Gulf outflows. This report documents the organization of the data set and the method of quality control used to eliminate unrealistic data. Also, it provides  a summary in graphic form of the hydrographic  observations.</dc:description>

<dc:title>R/V Seward Johnson cruise report (SJ-9807), ACCE S-PALACE float deployments</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Montgomery, Ellyn T.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Guest, Brian J.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Neutrally buoyant floats</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Tropical circulation</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Salinity profiling</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>ALACE</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Seward Johnson (Ship) Cruise SJ98-07</dc:subject>
<dc:description>R/V Seward Johnson cruise 98-07 occurred between October 12 and November 5, 1998. The goal of the cruise was to deploy 30 Salinity Profiling Autonomous Lagrangian Current Explorer (S-PALACE) floats in the tropical Atlantic as part of the Atlantic Circulation and Climate Experiment (ACCE). These floats are neutrally buoyant and drift with the water in which they are deployed. They are programmed to obtain temperature and salinity profiles of the top 1000 meters of the ocean every ten days. To ascertain the validity of the float data, a CTD profile was made at the site and time of deployment of each float.&#xd;
The data from these floats augments data already being obtained from ten floats deployed last year. Given 40 floats in the tropical North Atlantic, reporting every 10 to 14 days with an expected operational life of 3-5 years, we hope to gather 5000 to 7000 profiles from these floats in the coming years. The temperature and salinity profiles, along with derived properties will aid in examining the mechanisms of upper ocean heat and freshwater storage. This data, combined with satellite sea surface should provide useful input to climate and weather prediction models.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Science Foundation under Grant No. OCE-831869.</dc:description>

<dc:title>RAFOS float trajectories in Meddies during the Semaphore Experiment, 1993-1995</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Richardson, Philip L.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Wooding, Christine M.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Meddies</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>RAFOS floats</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Semaphore</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Ocean currents</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Mediterranean Sea</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Charles Darwin (Ship) Cruise</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Alcyon (Ship) Cruise</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Laperouse (Ship) Cruise</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Ailette (Ship) Cruise</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>D'Entrecasteaux (Ship) Cruise</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Suroit (Ship) Cruise</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Pr. Stockman (Ship) Cruise</dc:subject>
<dc:description>As part of the Semaphore Experiment four Meddies (Mediterranean Water Eddies) were discovered in the Canary Basin and tracked with freely drifting RAFOS floats. An additional Meddy was discovered off Lisbon by Pingree (1995) and also tracked with RAFOS floats. One large and energetic Meddy, discovered 1700 km west of Cape St. Vincent, Portugal, set a distance and speed record as it translated another 1700 km southwestward at 3.9 cm/sec during the 1.5 years. This Meddy traveled 57% of the distance from Cape St. Vincent toward the spot McDowell and Rossby (1978) found a possible Meddy north of the Dominican Republic. Four Meddies collided with tall seamounts which seemed to disrupt the normal swirl velocity perhaps fatally in three cases. One Meddy appeared to bifurcate when it collided with seamounts. This report describes the float trajectories in the Meddies and summarizes the main results.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Science Foundation under contract number OCE 93-01234.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Pan American Climate Study (PACS) : mooring recovery cruise report, R/V Melville cruise PACS03MV, 6 September to 30 September 1998</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Ostrom, William M.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Way, Bryan S.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Anderson, Steven P.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Jones, Brent</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Key, Erica</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Yuras, Gabriel</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Air-sea interaction</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Moored instrument measurements</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>PACS</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Pan American Climate Study</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Eastern tropical Pacific</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Ocean temperature</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Melville (Ship) Cruise PACS03MV</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Two surface moorings were recovered during R/V Melville cruise PACS03MV in the eastern equatorial Pacific as part of the Pan American Climate Study (PACS). PACS is a NOAA-funded study with the goal of investigating links between sea-surface temperature variability in the tropical oceans near the Americas and climate over the  American continents. The two moorings were deployed near 125°W, spanning the strong meridional sea-surface temperature gradient associated with the cold tongue south of the equator and the warmer ocean north of the equator, near the northernmost, summer location of the Inter-tropical Convergence Zone. The moored array was deployed to improve the understanding of air-sea fluxes  and of the processes that control the evolution of the sea surface temperature field in the region.&#xd;
Two surface mooring, located at 3°S, 125°W and 10°N, 125°W, belonging to the Upper Ocean Proccess (UOP) Group at the Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution (WHOI), were recovered after being on station for eight months. This was the second setting of the two moorings that had been redeployed from the University of Washington's R/V Thomas Thompson cruise number 73.&#xd;
The buoys of the two WHOI moorings were each equipped with meteorological instrumentation, including a Vector Averaging Wind Recorder (VAWR), and an Improved METeorological (IMET) system. The WHOI moorings also carried Vector Measuring Current Meters, single point temperature recorders, and conductivity and temperature recorders located in the upper 200 meters of the mooring line. In addition to the instrumentation noted above, a variety of other instruments, including an acoustic current meter, acoustic doppler meters, bio-optical instrument packages and an acoustic rain gauge, were deployed during the PACS field program.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration under contract number NA66GPO130.</dc:description>

<dc:description>A workshop entitled "Internal Solitary Waves in the Ocean: Their Physics and Implications for Acoustics, Biology, and Geology" was held during October, 1998 in Sydney, British Columbia, Canada. It was jointly organized by the Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution (MA, USA), the Institute of Ocean Sciences, (Sydney, BC, Canada), and the U. S. Office of Naval Research.  More than 60 scientists from seven countries attended. Participants contributed papers prior to the meeting which were published on the internet at the Woods Hole web site.  Those papers are reproduced here.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Digital data logging and processing, Derbyshire Survey, 1997</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Howland, Jonathan C.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Derbyshire (ship)</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Shipwrecks</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Automatic data collection systems</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Information storage and retrieval systems</dc:subject>
<dc:description>In 1997, the Deep Submergence Group (DSG) of the Woods Hole Oceanographic&#xd;
Institution (WHOI) surveyed the wreckage field of the M.V. Derbyshire. The motivation&#xd;
for the survey and its results are described elsewhere (Williams et al, 1998). The purpose&#xd;
of this report is to describe the digital data logging and processing systems that were used by the Deep Submergence Group during the survey. The report is divided into four&#xd;
sections: this Introduction, a description of the collection mechanisms, a description of the processing schemes and series of appendices. The appendices include a glossary of terms, a description of data formats, and a comparison of electronic still camera&#xd;
processing choices. Readers desiring information on the equipment used, on the&#xd;
operations, or on the analysis effort performed by the on-board Inspection and&#xd;
Verification (I &amp; V) Team or by the Assessors ashore are directed to (Williams et al, 1998),&#xd;
(Ballard, 1993) and (Bowen, et al, 1993).</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Science Foundation under Grant No. OCE-9627160&#xd;
and a Memorandum of Agreement between the United States Government and the United Kingdom Department of the Environment, Transport and the Regions.</dc:description>

<dc:title>A shallow-water model for hydraulically transcritical flows</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Rogerson, A. M.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Shallow water</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Numerical model</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Shock-capturing</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Water waves</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Ocean curents</dc:subject>
<dc:description>This document describes a numerical model that was developed to study two-dimensional,&#xd;
reduced-gravity, shallow-water flows. When the dynamics of these flows is strongly nonlinear, the flow may become hydraulically supercritical and discontinuities in the flow field may arise. The presence of discontinuities in the flow field requires a special numerical treatment in order to maintain both accuracy and stability in the numerically-approximated solution.&#xd;
In this model, a shock-capturing scheme called the Essentially Non-Oscillatory (ENO)&#xd;
scheme is implemented. The ENO scheme is a high-order, adaptive-stencil, finite-difference,&#xd;
characteristic-based scheme for hyperbolic equations that has been applied widely to flows governed by the Euler equations of gas dynamics. The model described in this document was developed for geophysical applications, and therefore includes the effects of rotation (constant Coriolis parameter), forcing (time dependent and/or spatially varying), and bottom&#xd;
drag (linear or nonlinear). The presentation includes the mathematical formulation of&#xd;
the model as well as instructions on how to prepare and execute model runs.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Evaluation of NSCAT scatterometer winds using equatorial Pacific buoy observations</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Caruso, Michael J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Dickinson, Suzanne</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Kelly, Kathryn A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Spillane, Mick</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Mangum, Linda J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>McPhaden, Michael J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Stratton, Linda D.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Scatterometer</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Buoy</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Calibration validation</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Wind waves</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Radar meteorology</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>NSCAT</dc:subject>
<dc:description>As part of the calibration/validation effort for NASA's Scatterometer (NSCAT) we compare&#xd;
the satellite data to winds measured at the sea surface with an array of buoys moored in the equatorial Pacific Ocean. The NSCAT data record runs from September, 1996 through the&#xd;
end of June, 1997. The raw NSCAT data, radar backscatter, is converted to wind vectors at&#xd;
10 meters above the surface assuming a neutrally stratified atmosphere, using the NSCAT-1 and NSCAT-2 model functions. The surface winds were measured directly by the TAO (Tropical Atmosphere Ocean) buoy array which spans the width of the equatorial Pacific within about 8° of the equator. The buoy program and data archive are maintained by the Pacific Marine Environmental Laboratory, at the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration,&#xd;
in collaboration with institutions in Japan, France and Taiwan. We also use data from two&#xd;
buoys maintained by the Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution located along 125°W. Since the buoy winds are measured at various heights above the surface, they are adjusted for both height and atmospheric surface layer stratification before comparisons are made to the NSCAT data. Co-location requirements include measurements within 100 km and 60 minutes&#xd;
of each other. There was a total of 5580 comparisons for the NSCAT-1 model function and 6364 comparisons for the NSCAT-2 model function. The NSCAT wind speeds, using the&#xd;
NSCAT-1 model function, are lower than the buoy wind speeds by about 0.54 ms-1 and have&#xd;
a 9.8° directional bias. The NSCAT-2 winds speeds were lower than the TAO buoy winds by&#xd;
only 0.08 ms-1, but still have the same 9.8° directional bias. The wind retrieval algorithm selects the vector closest to the buoy approximately 88% of the time. However, in the relatively&#xd;
low wind speed regime of the TAO array, approximately 4% of the wind vectors are more than 120° from the buoy wind.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Aeronautics and Space Administration&#xd;
under Contract No. 957652.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Navigation for the Derbyshire Phase2 Survey</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Lerner, Steven A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Yoerger, Dana R.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Crook, T.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Derbyshire (ship)</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Underwater navigation</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Jason (remotely operated vehicle)</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Argo (towed vehicle)</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>DSL 120</dc:subject>
<dc:description>In 1997, the Deep Submergence Operations Group of the Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution conducted an underwater forensic survey of the UK bulk-carrier MV Derbyshire with a suite of underwater vehicles. This report describes the navigation systems and methodologies used to precisely position the vessel and vehicles. Precise navigation permits the survey team to control the path of the subsea vehicle in order to execute the  survey plan, provides the ability to return to specific targets, and allows the assessment team to correlate observations made at different times from different vehicles. In this report, we summarize the techniques used to locate Argo as well as the  repeatability of those navigation fixes. To determine repeatability, we selected a number of instances where the vehicle lines crossed. By registering two images from overlapping areas on different tracklines, we can determine the true position offset. By comparing the position offset derived from the images to the offsets obtained from navigation, we can determine the navigation error. The average error for 123 points across a single tie line was 3.1 meters, the average error for a more scattered selection of 18 points was 1.9 meters.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Science Foundation under Grant No. OCE-9627160 and a Memorandum of Agreement between the United States Government and the United Kingdom Department of the Environment, Transport and the Regions.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Ice-ocean environmental buoy program : archived data processing and graphical results from April 1992 through November 1998</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Krishfield, Richard A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Honjo, Susumu</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Takizawa, Takatoshi</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Hatakeyama, Kiyoshi</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Arctic buoy</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Air data</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Ice data</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Ocean data</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Environmental observations</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Ocean-atmosphere interaction</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Sverdrup II (Ship) Cruise</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Des Grosilliers (Ship) Cruise</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Louis S. St. Laurent (Ship) Cruise</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Between 1992 and 1998, three Ice-Ocean Environmental Buoys (IOEBs) were deployed a total of six times on multiyear pack ice in the Arctic Ocean. The processing scheme for the telemetered environmental data, as well as the individual IOEBs and field operations, are described, and the processed data are presented in graphical form. &#xd;
The IOEB Archived Data Processing (IADP) processing scheme was conceived specifically to remove noise in data telemetered from IOEBs caused by errors in network or satellite communications, and to calibrate to absolute values. The location data consists of Argos quality 2 or 3 positions from each platform transmit terminal (PTT) on a particular IOEB, which are subsequently screened, interpolated, and combined with similarly processed locations from the other PTT, and further smoothed with a 6-hr triangular filter to produce an hourly timeseries with a standard error estimated to be -150 m. The sensor data is prefiltered, combined, adjusted for buoy drift, and screened with a Gaussian first difference filter to produce unevenly spaced timeseries of each variable measured by the IOEB. Every variable is output to a unique file per year, per buoy consisting of a two-column ACSII timeseries.&#xd;
Drift and sensor data are presented from the 1992 Transpolar Drift IOEB which operated for only 4 months, IOEB-1 which drifted with the Beaufort Gyre in a large anticyclonic circle from April 1992 to November 1998, and IOEB-2 which drifted through the Fram Strait in 9 months in 1994, and 1997-98 was redeployed concurrently with SHEBA at a distance 50 km from the main camp.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the Office of Naval Research under contract number N00014-99-10335 and Japan Marine Science and Technology Center.</dc:description>

<dc:description>Visual is a visualization system used to access and analyze high-volume multi-sensor data collected from remotely operated underwater vehicles.  Since 1991, scientists have used Visual for scientific visualization and analysis of underwater surveys ranging from real-time survey monitoring, to geological mapping and interpretation of hydrothermal vent sites, to a forensic study of a shipwreck. This report describes Visual's capabilities and gives examples of typical applications for Visual including sonar visualization, real-time monitoring, and multi-sensor data access and analysis. This report also includes a User's Manual and Reference Guide for the Visual system.</dc:description>

<dc:title>The horizontal mooring : a two-dimensional array, description of the array, components, instrumentation, deployment and recovery operations</dc:title>

<dc:description>A moored two-dimensional array with instrumentation distributed both horizontally&#xd;
and vertically was deployed for 27 days in August 1998 at an 85 meter deep site in&#xd;
Massachusetts Bay near Stellwagon basin. The horizontal mooring consisted of a 160-&#xd;
meter long horizontal element positioned at a depth of 20 meters between two subsurface&#xd;
moorings. Suspended below the horizontal member were five 25-meter long vertical&#xd;
strings. The vertical strings had a horizontal separation of 30 meters and each had instruments&#xd;
at depths of 20, 25, 30, 35, 40 and 45 meters. Instrumentation deployed on the&#xd;
two-dimensional array included acoustic current meters, temperature sensors, conductivity&#xd;
measuring instruments, pressure sensors and motion monitoring packages.&#xd;
This report includes a detailed description of the two-dimensional array, the anchoring&#xd;
system and the instrumentation that were deployed. Also included is a description&#xd;
of the deployment and recovery techniques that were employed as well as an assessment&#xd;
of the performance of the array.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Turbulence and waves over irregularly sloping topography : cruise report - Oceanus 324</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Montgomery, Ellyn T.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Polzin, Kurt L.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Turbulent mixing</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Rough bathymetry</dc:subject>

<dc:description>This report documents the work of R/V Oceanus cruise 324, which occurred during May of 1998. &#xd;
This cruise was the field component of the Turbulence and Waves in Irregularly Sloping&#xd;
Topography (TWIST) program. TWIST was part of the Littoral Internal Wave Initiative (LIW)&#xd;
supported by the Office of Naval Research.&#xd;
The objective of TWIST was to sample the background, internal wave and turbulence properties&#xd;
on the Continental Slope in the Mid-Atlantic Bight. Previous investigations have revealed&#xd;
strongly enhanced finescale internal wavefields and much more energetic turbulence due to&#xd;
internal wave breaking above topographic roughness associated with the Mid-Atlantic Ridge. So,&#xd;
an area of steeply sloping ridges and troughs running perpendicular to the continental slope near&#xd;
36˚34'N, 74˚39'W was chosen as the site of the observational program due to its topographic&#xd;
similarity to the Mid-Atlantic Ridge.&#xd;
Fíve instrument systems were employed to make observations during this cruise: the High&#xd;
Resolution Profier (HRP), three Moored Profiler (MP) moorings, a Lowered Acoustic Doppler&#xd;
Current Profiler/Conductivity, Temperature, Depth (LADCP/CTD) rosette, eXpendable Current&#xd;
Profilers/eXpendable CTD (XCP/XCTD), and finally, the shipboard ADCP. The data from these&#xd;
instruments (more than 1100 full depth profiles) provide adequate spatial and temporal resolution&#xd;
to describe the finescale and turbulent processes observed.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Electronic still camera processing and mosaicking</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Howland, Jonathan C.</dc:creator>

<dc:subject>Electronic equipment</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Digital techniques</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Since 1990, the Deep Submergence Laboratory and  Deep Submergence Group of the Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution have been collecting large quantities of digital imagery and creating digital photomosaics of the sea floor. Initially, the digital image collection, processing, and mosaicking processes were all highly specialized "one of a kind" efforts. Over the past decade, this process has been refined, standardized, and made into a robust "pipeline." The collection , processing, and mosaicking can be reliably carried out on a routine basis. Deep Submergence Group personnel perform collection, processing, and archiving, while science party users can be trained in mosaicking. This report will describe the pipeline, yielding insight into the evolution and purposes of each step.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the Office of Naval Research under Contract No. N00014-95-1-1237 and by the National Science Foundation under Grant No. OCE-9627160.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Stirring and mixing : 1999 Program of Summer Study in Geophysical Fluid Dynamics</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Balmforth, Neil J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Young, William R.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Fields, Janet</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Thiffeault, Jean-Luc</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Pasquero, Claudia</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Fluids</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Mixing</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Transport</dc:subject>
<dc:description>The central theme of the 1999 GFD Program was the stirring, transport, reaction and mixing of passive and active tracers in&#xd;
turbulent, stratified, rotating fluids. The problem of mixing in fluids has applications in areas ranging from oceanography to&#xd;
engineering and astrophysics. In geophysical settings, mixing spans and unites a broad range of scales -- from micrometers to&#xd;
megameters. The mixing of passive tracers is of fundamental importance in environmental and industrial problems, such as&#xd;
pollution, and in determining the large-scale heat and salt balance of the worlds oceans. The transport of active tracers, on the other&#xd;
hand, such as vorticity, plays a key role in the turbulence that occurs in most geophysical and astrophysical fluids. William R.&#xd;
Young (Scripps Institution of Oceanography) gave a series of principal lectures, the notes of which as taken by the fellows, appear&#xd;
in this volume. Report of the projects of the student fellows makes up the second half of this volume.</dc:description>

the Office of Naval Research under Grant No. NOO0l4-97-1-0934.</dc:description>

<dc:title>North Brazil Current Rings Experiment : surface drifter data report, November 1998-June 2000</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Glickson, Deborah A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Fratantoni, David M.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Wooding, Christine M.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Richardson, Philip L.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Tropical Atlantic circulation</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Mesoscale rings</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Satellite-tracked drifters</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Ocean currents</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Seward Johnson (Ship) Cruise NBC 98</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Seward Johnson (Ship) Cruise NBC 99</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Seward Johnson (Ship) Cruise NBC 00</dc:subject>
<dc:description>This data report summarzes 45 surface drifter trajectories collected between&#xd;
November 1998 and June 2000 as part of the North Brazil Current (NBC) Rings Experiment.&#xd;
NBC rings have been proposed as one of several important mechanisms for the transport of&#xd;
South Atlantic upper-ocean water across the equatorial-tropical gyre boundary and into the&#xd;
North Atlantic subtropical gyre. Such transport is required to complete the meridional&#xd;
overturning cell in the Atlantic forced by the high-latitude production and southward export of&#xd;
North Atlantic Deep Water. The goal of this program is to obtain, for the first time,&#xd;
comprehensive observations of the NBC retroflection, the NBC ring formation process, and&#xd;
the physical structure and properties of NBC rings as they translate northwestward along the&#xd;
low-latitude western boundary. A total of 45 drifters were deployed. Twenty-four of these&#xd;
looped anticyclonically within the five rings identified during this experiment. Seven of the&#xd;
looping ring drifters entered the Caribbean, while the rest moved northward along the eastern&#xd;
flank of the Lesser Antiles.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Science Foundation under Grant No. OCE-9729765.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Estimated annual economic impacts from harmful algal blooms (HABs) in the United States</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Anderson, Donald M.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Hoagland, Porter</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Kaoru, Yoshi</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>White, Alan W.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Harmful algal blooms</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>HABs</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Red tides</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Economic impacts</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Brown tides</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>United States</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Blooms of toxic or harmful microalgae, commonly called "red tides," represent a significant and expanding threat to human&#xd;
health and fisheries resources throughout the United States and the world. Ecological, aesthetic, and public health impacts&#xd;
include: mass mortalities of wild and farmed fish and shellfish, human intoxication and death from the consumption of&#xd;
contaminated shellfish or fish, alterations of marine food webs through adverse effects on larvae and other life history stages of&#xd;
commercial fish species, the noxious smell and appearance of algae accumulated in nearshore waters or deposited on beaches, and&#xd;
mass mortalities of marine mammals, seabirds, and other animals.&#xd;
In this report, we provide an estimate of the economic impacts of HABs in the United States from events where such impacts&#xd;
were measurable with a fair degree of confidence during the interval 1987-92. The total economic impact averaged $49 million&#xd;
per year, with public health impacts representing the largest component (45 percent). Commercial fisheries impacts were the next&#xd;
largest (37 percent of the total), while recreation/tourism accounted for 13 percent, and monitoring/management impacts 4 percent.&#xd;
These estimates are highly conservative, as many economic costs or impacts from HABs could not be estimated.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the National Oceanic and Atmospheric&#xd;
Administration under Grants No. NA46RG0470 and NA90AA-D-SG480, the&#xd;
National Science Foundation under Grant No. OCE-9321244, and the Johnson&#xd;
Endowment of the Marine Policy Center.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Southern Ocean GLOBEC moored array and automated weather station data report</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Moffat, Carlos F.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Beardsley, Robert C.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Limeburner, Richard</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Owens, W. Brechner</dc:creator>

<dc:creator>Hyatt, Jason</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>SO GLOBEC</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Antarctica</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Coastal oceanography</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Laurence M. Gould (Ship) Cruise LMG01-03</dc:subject>

<dc:description>Southern Ocean GLOBal ocean ECosystems</dc:description>
<dc:description>As part of the U.S. Southern Ocean GLOBEC program, moored time series measurements of&#xd;
temperature, conductivity (salinity), pressure, velocity, and acoustic backscatter were made from&#xd;
March 2001 to March 2003 in and near Marguerite Bay, located on the Antarctic Peninsula western&#xd;
shelf. To monitor surface forcing during the moored array observations, two automatic weather&#xd;
stations (AWSs) were deployed on islands in Marguerite Bay and time series of wind, air temperature,&#xd;
pressure, and relative humidity were collected from May 2001 through March 2003. This&#xd;
report describes the individual moorings, their locations and local bathymetry, the instrumentation&#xd;
used and measurement depths, calibration and data processing steps taken to produce final time&#xd;
series, and basic plots of the final time series. The AWS data acquisition and processing are also&#xd;
described and basic plots of the final meteorological time series presented. Directions are given&#xd;
about how to access the raw and processed moored and AWS data via the SO GLOBEC website&#xd;
(http://globec.whoi.edu/jg/dir/globec/soglobec/).</dc:description>

<dc:title>2004 program of study : tides</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Balmforth, Neil J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Llewellyn-Smith, Stefan</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Hendershott, Myrl</dc:creator>

<dc:subject>Internal waves</dc:subject>

<dc:description>The summer of 2004 saw the GFD program tackle “Tides”. Myrl Hendershott (Scripps&#xd;
Institution of Oceanography) gave a fabulous introduction to the subject in the first week&#xd;
of the course, laying the foundations from astronomy and classical geophysical&#xd;
fluid dynamics. In the second week, Chris Garrett (University of Victoria) admirably followed&#xd;
up with recent developments on the subject, including the recent observations from satellite&#xd;
altimetry, their implications to mixing and circulation, and even a memorable lecture on&#xd;
the noble theme of how we might solve the world's energy crisis. The principal lectures&#xd;
proved unusually popular this summer, and the seminar room at Walsh often overflowed in the&#xd;
first two weeks.&#xd;
Following on from the lectures, the seminar schedule of the summer covered in greater&#xd;
detail the oceanographic issues with which researchers are actively grappling. We also&#xd;
heard about related problems regarding atmospheric, planetary and stellar tides, together&#xd;
with the usual mix of topics on GFD in general.&#xd;
The summer once again featured a lecture for the general public in the Woods Hole&#xd;
area. Carl Wunsch delivered a very well received lecture entitled “Climate Change Stories”,&#xd;
in which he gave an impression of how scientists generally believe our climate is currently&#xd;
changing, whilst simultaneously urging caution against some of the more outrageous and&#xd;
exaggerated claims. The lecture was held at Lilly Auditorium, thanks to the hospitality&#xd;
of the Marine Biology Laboratory. The reception following the lecture was enjoyed by&#xd;
all.&#xd;
Neil Balmforth and Stefan Llewellyn Smith acted as Co-Directors for the summer.&#xd;
Janet Fields, Jeanne Fleming and Penny Foster provided the administrative backbone to&#xd;
the Program, both during the summer and throughout the year beforehand. As always,&#xd;
we were grateful to the Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution for the use of Walsh Cottage,&#xd;
and Keith Bradley's solid service could not be overlooked. Shilpa Ghadge and Shreyas&#xd;
Mandre are to be thanked for their part in comforting the fellows, developing the summer's&#xd;
proceedings volume (available on the GFD web site) and for running the computer network.</dc:description>

<dc:title>A key to the stromateoid fishes</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Haedrich, Richard L.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Horn, Michael H.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Fishes</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Stromateoid</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Keys</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Perciformes</dc:subject>
<dc:description>This second edition of "A Key to the Stromateoid Fishes" is a partially&#xd;
up-dated version of the original (W.H.O.I. Ref. No. 69-70, September 1969).</dc:description>
<dc:description>Our primary purpose in preparing "A Key to the Stromateoid&#xd;
Fishes" is to provide field workers and curators with a convenient&#xd;
and concise aid for the identification of the diverse species in this&#xd;
somewhat difficult group. &#xd;
Secondarily, we hope to present, through&#xd;
the keys, a summary of the present state of our knowledge of these&#xd;
fishes, and to indicate areas where further investigation is needed.&#xd;
The keys which compose this handbook have been derived from&#xd;
several sources. Some are slightly modified from already published&#xd;
or about-to-be-published sources.&#xd;
Others form a part of manuscripts&#xd;
in preparation. A third group of keys has&#xd;
been constructed from published&#xd;
species descriptions and our own often meagre data.&#xd;
The keys are intended primarily for larger specimens.&#xd;
Small stromateoids are particularly confusing, and it is not our purpose&#xd;
to treat them here. The well-known and marked allometric growth in&#xd;
stromateoids remains a problem, and those who use these keys should&#xd;
be aware that the body proportions&#xd;
of very large and/or very small&#xd;
specimens can fall well outside the limits here set.&#xd;
We expect and&#xd;
hope for corrections and improvements to the keys, and have left&#xd;
them double-spaced so comments&#xd;
may be written in by users.&#xd;
We have&#xd;
not attempted to settle nomenclatural problems, but have used the oldest&#xd;
name we know of when a choice is necessary.&#xd;
Neither have we&#xd;
gone into the problem of synonymy to any great extent.&#xd;
In some cases&#xd;
we have approached this problem by including two names under one&#xd;
couplet in a key. The choice of which name to employ is thus passed&#xd;
on to the user.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Prepared for the National Science Foundation&#xd;
under Grants GA-31365X, GB-710B, and GZ-259.</dc:description>
<dc:date>2005-11-08T15:05:32Z</dc:date>
<dc:date>2005-11-08T15:05:32Z</dc:date>
