<dc:title>Tidal circulation and flushing characteristics of the Nauset Marsh System : report to the Town of Orleans</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Aubrey, David G.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Voulgaris, George</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Spencer, Wayne D.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>O'Malley, Stephen P.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Tidal flushing</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Water quality</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Numerical modeling</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Salt marshes</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Water pollution</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Various interested bodies (i.e., National Park Servce, Cape Cod Commssion, and the&#xd;
Town of Orleans) charged with management of the Nauset Marsh system on Cape Cod, MA,&#xd;
commissioned a study of the estuarine circulation within the Nauset system. Recent signficant&#xd;
morphological changes in the system have changed mixing processes and residence times for the&#xd;
embayment. This study specifically addressed the differing water circulation and residence times&#xd;
arising from a migrating single inlet (dominant condition) and dual inlet (1992-1996) situations.&#xd;
These residence times are to be used by the Cape Cod Commission to identify nitrogen-sensitive&#xd;
sub-embayments based on various assumptions of build-out and nutrient loading. The Nauset&#xd;
Marsh system has experienced considerable development in recent years; proper management of&#xd;
this resource area requires knowledge of the consequences of such development.&#xd;
Application of field observations of bathymetry, sea surface elevation, temperature,&#xd;
salinity and currents, leads to better understanding the physics of the system. These data,&#xd;
analyzed in various forms, served as input data for a numerical, two-dimensional circulation&#xd;
model of the embayment. The circulation model provided flow and discharge data with which the&#xd;
residence times were calculated. Bathymetric measurements defined the volumes of the various&#xd;
sub-embayments to be used in the calculation of residence times.&#xd;
Residence times were calculated for six sub-embayments of the system, defined on the&#xd;
basis of their common hydrodynamic and morphologic characteristics. Two scenarios were&#xd;
evaluated: one for the present single-inlet system, which is near typical for most system states,&#xd;
and one for a dual inlet system such as existed for a period of time from 1992 through 1996.&#xd;
Residence times were evaluated for twelve cases, to demonstrate the range of residence&#xd;
times that can be defined based on varing assumptions. For instance, residence times can be&#xd;
defined on the basis of mean low water volumes or mean water levels, the latter being the more&#xd;
conservative (yielding a longer residence time). In addition, residence times depend on whether&#xd;
spring tides, neap tides, or average tidal conditions are used. We provide data on all three&#xd;
conditions: the neap tidal case is the most conservative in the sense of providing a longer&#xd;
residence time. This case can serve as the basis for flushing if conservatism is desired. Finally,&#xd;
residence time can be defined based on the amount of time it takes for water to renew itself with&#xd;
water from adjacent sub-embayments, or more conservatively assuming renewal from the&#xd;
offshore waters (which are presumed to be cleaner).&#xd;
Based on these various inputs, assumptions and calculations, residence times for Salt and&#xd;
Mill ponds under conditions of a single inlet are the longest of the various sub-embayments.&#xd;
Town Cove is still relatively quickly renewed, though not as fast as the main channels serving the&#xd;
system.&#xd;
Flow pattern under dual-inlet condition does seem to be partioned well, with the&#xd;
northern inlet serving the northern part of the system and the southern inlet serving the southern&#xd;
part of the system, with litte hydrodynamc communication between the two divisions. This&#xd;
new hydrodynamc behavior results in shorter residence times under dual inlets than under a&#xd;
single inlet.&#xd;
Calculations indicate that the slowest flushing occurs in Mill and Salt ponds. The main&#xd;
body of the embayment, consisting of narrow channels between well-flushed salt marsh and tidal&#xd;
flats, flushes rapidly. Two-dimensional calculations show that Town Cove also flushes&#xd;
relatively rapidly, on average. However, its greater depth and occasional temperature&#xd;
stratification create conditions which might accumulate nutrients in bottom sediments, which,&#xd;
when released, can cause decrease in water quality (such as plankton blooms). A more&#xd;
sophisticated low-trophic level ecosystem model combined with vertical hydrodynamic structure&#xd;
could clarfy the dynamics of this process.&#xd;
This study provides a defensible basis for evaluating nutrient loading and potential&#xd;
eutrophication arising from development in the watershed around Nauset embayment. However,&#xd;
since morphological changes occur on a rapid basis in this area, the issue of residence time should&#xd;
be re-examined periodically. For instance, rapid onshore migration of the southern barrier beach&#xd;
is threatening closure of the south chanel, a condition which could adversely affect water quality&#xd;
in Nauset Harbor in the near futue. A process should be established to examine the sensitivity&#xd;
of residence times for rapidly changing morphology.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Funding was provided by the Town of Orleans, the National Park Service&#xd;
and the Andrew W. Mellon Foundation.</dc:description>

<dc:creator>Wernegreen, Jennifer J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Moran, Nancy A.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Buchnera aphidicola</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Aphids</dc:subject>

<dc:description>Author Posting. © American Society for Microbiology, 2001.  This article is posted here by permission of American Society for Microbiology for personal use, not for redistribution. <dc:description>This study tested for horizontal transfer of plasmids among Buchnera aphidicola strains associated with&#xd;
ecologically and phylogenetically related aphid hosts (Uroleucon species). Phylogenetic congruence of Buchnera&#xd;
plasmid (trpEG and leuABC) and chromosomal (dnaN and trpB) genes supports strictly vertical long-term transmission&#xd;
of plasmids, which persist due to their contributions to host nutrition rather than capacity for infectious&#xd;
transfer. Synonymous divergences indicate elevated mutation on plasmids relative to chromosomal genes.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Role of carbon cycle observations and knowledge in carbon management</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Dilling, Lisa</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Doney, Scott C.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Edmonds, Jae</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Gurney, Kevin R.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Harriss, Robert</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Schimel, David S.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Stephens, Britton B.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Stokes, Gerald</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Carbon sequestration</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Measurement techniques</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Climate</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Kyoto protocol</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Author Posting. © Annual Reviews, 2003.  This article is posted here by permission of Annual Reviews for personal use, not for redistribution.  The definitive version was published in Annual Review of Environment and Resources 28 (2003): 521-558, doi:10.1146/annurev.energy.28.011503.163443.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Agriculture and industrial development have led to inadvertent changes&#xd;
in the natural carbon cycle. As a consequence, concentrations of carbon dioxide and other greenhouse gases have increased in the atmosphere and may lead to changes in&#xd;
climate. The current challenge facing society is to develop options for future management&#xd;
of the carbon cycle. A variety of approaches has been suggested: direct reduction&#xd;
of emissions, deliberate manipulation of the natural carbon cycle to enhance sequestration,&#xd;
and capture and isolation of carbon from fossil fuel use. Policy development to&#xd;
date has laid out some of the general principles to which carbon management should&#xd;
adhere. These are summarized as: how much carbon is stored, by what means, and&#xd;
for how long. To successfully manage carbon for climate purposes requires increased&#xd;
understanding of carbon cycle dynamics and improvement in the scientific capabilities&#xd;
available for measurement as well as for policy needs. The specific needs for scientific&#xd;
information to underpin carbon cycle management decisions are not yet broadly&#xd;
known. A stronger dialogue between decision makers and scientists must be developed&#xd;
to foster improved application of scientific knowledge to decisions. This review focuses&#xd;
on the current knowledge of the carbon cycle, carbon measurement capabilities&#xd;
(with an emphasis on the continental scale) and the relevance of carbon cycle science&#xd;
to carbon sequestration goals.</dc:description>
<dc:description>The National Center for Atmospheric Research is supported by the National Science&#xd;
Foundation.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Comparative sequence analysis of IS50/Tn5 transposase</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Reznikoff, William S.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Bordenstein, Seth R.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Apodaca, Jennifer</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Transposase-related protein sequences</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Transposase function</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Author Posting. © American Society for Microbiology, 2004.  This article is posted here by permission of American Society for Microbiology for personal use, not for redistribution.  The definitive version was published in Journal of Bacteriology 186 (2004): 8240-8247, doi:10.1128/JB.186.24.8240-8247.2004.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Comparative sequence analysis of IS50 transposase-related protein sequences in conjunction with known&#xd;
structural, biochemical, and genetic data was used to determine domains and residues that play key roles in&#xd;
IS50 transposase function. BLAST and ClustalW analyses have been used to find and analyze six complete&#xd;
protein sequences that are related to the IS50 transposase. The protein sequence identity of these six homologs&#xd;
ranged from 25 to 55% in comparison to the IS50 transposase. Homologous motifs were found associated with&#xd;
each of the three catalytic residues. Residues that play roles in transposase-DNA binding, protein autoregulation,&#xd;
and DNA hairpin formation were also found to be conserved in addition to other residues of unknown&#xd;
function. On the other hand, some homologous sequences did not appear to be competent to encode the&#xd;
inhibitor regulatory protein. The results were also used to compare the IS50 transposase with the more&#xd;
distantly related transposase encoded by IS10.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Microbial biogeography along an estuarine salinity gradient : combined influences of bacterial growth and residence time</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Crump, Byron C.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Hopkinson, Charles S.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Sogin, Mitchell L.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Hobbie, John E.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Bacterioplankton community composition</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Parker River estuary</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Plum Island Sound</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Proteobacteria</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Bacteroidetes</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Actinobacteria</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Author Posting. © American Society for Microbiology, 2004.  This article is posted here by permission of American Society for Microbiology for personal use, not for redistribution.  The definitive version was published in Applied and Environmental Microbiology 70 (2004): 1494-1505, doi:10.1128/AEM.70.3.1494-1505.2004.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Shifts in bacterioplankton community composition along the salinity gradient of the Parker River estuary&#xd;
and Plum Island Sound, in northeastern Massachusetts, were related to residence time and bacterial community&#xd;
doubling time in spring, summer, and fall seasons. Bacterial community composition was characterized&#xd;
with denaturing gradient gel electrophoresis (DGGE) of PCR-amplified 16S ribosomal DNA. Average community&#xd;
doubling time was calculated from bacterial production ([14C]leucine incorporation) and bacterial&#xd;
abundance (direct counts). Freshwater and marine populations advected into the estuary represented a large&#xd;
fraction of the bacterioplankton community in all seasons. However, a unique estuarine community formed at&#xd;
intermediate salinities in summer and fall, when average doubling time was much shorter than water residence&#xd;
time, but not in spring, when doubling time was similar to residence time. Sequencing of DNA in DGGE bands&#xd;
demonstrated that most bands represented single phylotypes and that matching bands from different samples&#xd;
represented identical phylotypes. Most river and coastal ocean bacterioplankton were members of common&#xd;
freshwater and marine phylogenetic clusters within the phyla Proteobacteria, Bacteroidetes, and Actinobacteria.&#xd;
Estuarine bacterioplankton also belonged to these phyla but were related to clones and isolates from several&#xd;
different environments, including marine water columns, freshwater sediments, and soil.</dc:description>
<dc:description>This work was supported by two grants from the National Science&#xd;
Foundation (LTER grant OCE-9726921 and Microbial Observatory&#xd;
grant MCB-9977897) and the NASA Astrobiology Institute (cooperative&#xd;
agreement NCC2-1054 to M.L.S.).</dc:description>

<dc:title>A functional update of the Escherichia coli K-12 genome</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Serres, Margrethe H.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Gopal, Shuba</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Nahum, Laila A.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Liang, Ping</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Gaasterland, Terry</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Riley, Monica</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Escherichia coli K-12</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Author Posting. © 2001 Serres et al.  The definitive version was published in Genome Biology 2 (2001): research0035.1–0035.7, doi:10.1186/gb-2001-2-9-research0035.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Background: Since the genome of Escherichia coli K-12 was initially annotated in 1997, additional&#xd;
functional information based on biological characterization and functions of sequence-similar&#xd;
proteins has become available. On the basis of this new information, an updated version of the&#xd;
annotated chromosome has been generated.&#xd;
Results: The E. coli K-12 chromosome is currently represented by 4,401 genes encoding 116&#xd;
RNAs and 4,285 proteins. The boundaries of the genes identified in the GenBank Accession&#xd;
U00096 were used. Some protein-coding sequences are compound and encode multimodular&#xd;
proteins. The coding sequences (CDSs) are represented by modules (protein elements of at&#xd;
least 100 amino acids with biological activity and independent evolutionary history). There are&#xd;
4,616 identified modules in the 4,285 proteins. Of these, 48.9% have been characterized, 29.5%&#xd;
have an imputed function, 2.1% have a phenotype and 19.5% have no function assignment. Only&#xd;
7% of the modules appear unique to E. coli, and this number is expected to be reduced as more&#xd;
genome data becomes available. The imputed functions were assigned on the basis of manual&#xd;
evaluation of functions predicted by BLAST and DARWIN analyses and by the MAGPIE genome&#xd;
annotation system.&#xd;
Conclusions: Much knowledge has been gained about functions encoded by the E. coli K-12 genome&#xd;
since the 1997 annotation was published. The data presented here should be useful for analysis of&#xd;
E. coli gene products as well as gene products encoded by other genomes.</dc:description>
<dc:description>This work was supported by NIH grant RO1 RR07861, the NASA Astrobiology&#xd;
Institute grant NCC2-1054, grants from the Edward Mallinckrodt,&#xd;
Jr Foundation and the Sinsheimer Foundation, and NSF grants NSF DBI -&#xd;
9984882 and NSF IIS - 9996304.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Origin and significance of the Delaney Dome Formation, Connemara, Ireland</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Draut, Amy E.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Clift, Peter D.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Ireland</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Grampian Orogeny</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>U–Pb</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Geochemistry</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Subduction</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Plate collision</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Author Posting. © Geological Society of London, 2002.  This article is posted here by permission of Geological Society of London for personal use, not for redistribution.  The definitive version was published in Journal of the Geological Society 159 (2002): 95-103.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Dalradian meta-sediments of the Laurentian margin and mafic intrusions thereof in SW Connemara, Ireland, tectonically overlie meta-rhyolites of the Delaney Dome Formation. The two units are separated by the Mannin Thrust. A new U–Pb age of 474.6 ± 5.5 Ma shows that the Delaney Dome Formation is a temporal equivalent of arc volcanic rocks preserved in the adjacent South Mayo Trough: the Tourmakeady Volcanic Group, erupted during the collision of an oceanic island arc with the Laurentian margin in the Grampian Orogeny. New rare earth and high field strength element data show that the Delaney Dome Formation and Tourmakeady Volcanic Group are chemically similar and arc-like in character. This suggests that the Delaney Dome Formation is an along-strike equivalent of the Tourmakeady Group, strike-slip faulted south of the South Mayo Trough during or after the Grampian Orogeny. Further correlation of these units with northern Appalachian rhyolites is also possible. The Delaney Dome Formation is an extrusive temporal equivalent of intrusions that penetrate the Connemara Dalradian. Thus, movement along the Mannin Thrust brought mid-crustal plutons and Dalradian country rocks tectonically above the extrusive volcanic sequence. The Mannin Thrust is identified as a major imbricating structure within a continental arc, but not a terrane boundary.</dc:description>

<dc:date>2005-11-14T21:32:31Z</dc:date>

<dc:identifier>Journal of the Geological Society 159 (2002): 95-103</dc:identifier>

<dc:title>Gene expression level influences amino acid usage, but not codon usage, in the tsetse fly endosymbiont Wigglesworthia</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Herbeck, Joshua T.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Wall, Dennis P.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Wernegreen, Jennifer J.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Wigglesworthia glossinidia brevipalpis</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Synonymous codon usage</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Amino acid usage</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Glossina brevipalpis</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Author Posting. © Society for General Mircobiology, 2003.  This article is posted here by permission of Society for General Mircobiology for personal use, not for redistribution.  The definitive version was published in Microbiology 149 (2003): 2585-2596, doi:10.1099/mic.0.26381-0.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Wigglesworthia glossinidia brevipalpis, the obligate bacterial endosymbiont of the tsetse fly Glossina brevipalpis, is characterized by extreme genome reduction and AT nucleotide composition bias. Here, multivariate statistical analyses are used to test the hypothesis that mutational bias and genetic drift shape synonymous codon usage and amino acid usage of Wigglesworthia. The results show that synonymous codon usage patterns vary little across the genome and do not distinguish genes of putative high and low expression levels, thus indicating a lack of translational selection. Extreme AT composition bias across the genome also drives relative amino acid usage, but predicted high-expression genes (ribosomal proteins and chaperonins) use GC-rich amino acids more frequently than do low-expression genes. The levels and configuration of amino acid differences between Wigglesworthia and Escherichia coli were compared to test the hypothesis that the relatively GC-rich amino acid profiles of high-expression genes reflect greater amino acid conservation at these loci. This hypothesis is supported by reduced levels of protein divergence at predicted high-expression Wigglesworthia genes and similar configurations of amino acid changes across expression categories. Combined, the results suggest that codon and amino acid usage in the Wigglesworthia genome reflect a strong AT mutational bias and elevated levels of genetic drift, consistent with expected effects of an endosymbiotic lifestyle and repeated population bottlenecks. However, these impacts of mutation and drift are apparently attenuated by selection on amino acid composition at high-expression genes.</dc:description>
<dc:description>This work was made possible by support to&#xd;
J. J. W. from the NIH (R01 GM62626-01) and NSF (DEB 0089455),&#xd;
and the Josephine Bay Paul and C. Michael Paul Foundation.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Small genome of Candidatus Blochmannia, the bacterial endosymbiont of Camponotus, implies irreversible specialization to an intracellular lifestyle</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Wernegreen, Jennifer J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Lazarus, Adam B.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Degnan, Patrick H.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Genome reduction</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Symbiosis</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Bacteriocytes</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Asexuality</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Genetic drift</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Author Posting. © Society for General Mircobiology, 2002.  This article is posted here by permission of Society for General Mircobiology for personal use, not for redistribution.  The definitive version was published in Microbiology 148 (2002): 2551-2556.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Blochmannia (Candidatus Blochmannia gen. nov.) is the primary bacterial&#xd;
endosymbiont of the ant genus Camponotus. Like other obligate&#xd;
endosymbionts of insects, Blochmannia occurs exclusively within eukaryotic&#xd;
cells and has experienced long-term vertical transmission through host&#xd;
lineages. In this study, PFGE was used to estimate the genome size of&#xd;
Blochmannia as approximately 800 kb, which is significantly smaller than its&#xd;
free-living relatives in the enterobacteria. This small genome implies that&#xd;
Blochmannia has deleted most of the genetic machinery of related free-living&#xd;
bacteria. Due to restricted gene exchange in obligate endosymbionts, the&#xd;
substantial gene loss in Blochmannia and other insect mutualists may reflect&#xd;
irreversible specialization to a host cellular environment.</dc:description>
<dc:description>This work was supported by grants to J. J.W.&#xd;
from the National Institutes of Health (R01 GM62626-01), the&#xd;
National Science Foundation (DEB 0089455) and the&#xd;
Josephine Bay Paul and C. Michael Paul Foundation.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Teredinibacter turnerae gen. nov., sp. nov., a dinitrogen-fixing, cellulolytic, endosymbiotic c-proteobacterium isolated from the gills of wood-boring molluscs (Bivalvia: Teredinidae)</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Distel, Daniel L.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Morrill, Wendy</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>MacLaren-Toussaint, Noelle</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Franks, Diana G.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Waterbury, John B.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Teredinidae</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Bivalvia</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Shipworms</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Symbiont</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>16S rRNA phylogeny</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Author Posting. © Society for General Mircobiology, 2002.  This article is posted here by permission of Society for General Mircobiology for personal use, not for redistribution.  The definitive version was published in International Journal of Systematic Bacteriology 52 (2002): 2261-2269, doi:10.1099/ijs.0.02184-0.</dc:description>
<dc:description>A cellulolytic, dinitrogen-fixing bacterium isolated from the gill tissue of a&#xd;
wood-boring mollusc (shipworm) Lyrodus pedicellatus of the bivalve family&#xd;
Teredinidae and 58 additional strains with similar properties, isolated from&#xd;
gills of 24 bivalve species representing 9 of 14 genera of Teredinidae, are&#xd;
described. The cells are Gram-negative, rigid, rods (0&lt;4–0&lt;6x3–6 lm) that bear a&#xd;
single polar flagellum. All isolates are capable of chemoheterotrophic growth&#xd;
in a simple mineral medium supplemented with cellulose as a sole source of&#xd;
carbon and energy. Xylan, pectin, carboxymethylcellulose, cellobiose and a&#xd;
variety of sugars and organic acids also support growth. Growth requires&#xd;
addition of combined nitrogen when cultures are vigorously aerated, but all&#xd;
isolates fix dinitrogen under microaerobic conditions. The pH, temperature and&#xd;
salinity optima for growth were determined for six isolates and are&#xd;
approximately 8&lt;5, 30–35 °C and 0&lt;3 M NaCl respectively. The isolates are&#xd;
marine. In addition to NaCl, growth requires elevated concentrations of Ca2M&#xd;
and Mg2M that reflect the chemistry of seawater. The DNA GMC content ranged&#xd;
from 49 to 51 mol%. Four isolates were identical with respect to small-subunit&#xd;
rRNA sequence over 891 positions compared and fall within a unique clade in&#xd;
the c-subclass of the Proteobacteria. Based on morphological, physiological&#xd;
and phylogenetic characteristics and specific symbiotic association with&#xd;
teredinid bivalves, a new genus and species, Teredinibacter turnerae gen. nov.,&#xd;
sp. nov., is proposed. The type strain is T7902T (vATCC 39867TvDSM 15152T).</dc:description>
<dc:description>This work was supported by grants from the National&#xd;
Science Foundation no. NSF DEB-9420051 and IBN-&#xd;
9982982, the Maine Science and Technology Foundation's&#xd;
Center for Innovation in Biotechnology, and the University&#xd;
of Maine's Faculty Research program.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Endosymbiosis : lessons in conflict resolution</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Wernegreen, Jennifer J.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Endosymbiosis</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Endosymbiosis manipulation</dc:subject>
<dc:description>© 2004 Jennifer J. Wernegreen. This is an open-access article distributed under the terms of the Creative Commons Attribution License, which permits unrestricted use, distribution, and reproduction in any medium, provided the original work is properly cited. The definitive version was published in PLoS Biology 2 (2004): e68, doi:10.1371/journal.pbio.0020068.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Symbiosis, an interdependent&#xd;
relationship between two&#xd;
species, is an important driver&#xd;
of evolutionary novelty and ecological&#xd;
diversity. Microbial symbionts&#xd;
in particular have been major&#xd;
evolutionary catalysts throughout the&#xd;
4 billion years of life on earth and&#xd;
have largely shaped the evolution of&#xd;
complex organisms. Endosymbiosis is&#xd;
a specifi c type of symbiosis in which&#xd;
one—typically microbial—partner&#xd;
lives within its host and represents&#xd;
the most intimate contact between&#xd;
interacting organisms. Mitochondria&#xd;
and chloroplasts, for example, result&#xd;
from endosymbiotic events of lasting&#xd;
significance that extended the range&#xd;
of acceptable habitats for life. The&#xd;
wide distribution of intracellular&#xd;
bacteria across diverse hosts and marine and terrestrial habitats testifies&#xd;
to the continued importance of&#xd;
endosymbiosis in evolution.&#xd;
Among multicellular organisms,&#xd;
insects as a group form exceptionally&#xd;
diverse associations with microbial&#xd;
associates, including bacteria that&#xd;
live exclusively within host cells and&#xd;
undergo maternal transmission to&#xd;
offspring. These microbes have piqued&#xd;
the interest of evolutionary biologists&#xd;
because they represent a wide spectrum&#xd;
of evolutionary strategies, ranging from&#xd;
obligate mutualism to reproductive&#xd;
parasitism (Buchner 1965; Ishikawa&#xd;
2003) (Box 1; Table 1).</dc:description>
<dc:description>JJW gratefully acknowledges the support&#xd;
of the National Institutes of Health (R01&#xd;
GM62626-01), the National Science&#xd;
Foundation (DEB 0089455), the National&#xd;
Aeronautics and Space Administration&#xd;
Astrobiology Institute (NNA04CC04A), and&#xd;
the Josephine Bay Paul and C. Michael Paul&#xd;
Foundation.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Three Prochlorococcus cyanophage genomes : signature features and ecological interpretations</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Sullivan, Matthew B.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Coleman, Maureen L.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Weigele, Peter</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Rohwer, Forest</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Chisholm, Sallie W.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Oceanic cyanobacteria</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Prochlorococcus phages</dc:subject>
<dc:description>© 2005 Sullivan et al. This is an open-access article distributed under the terms of the Creative Commons Attribution License, which permits unrestricted use, distribution, and reproduction in any medium, provided the original work is properly cited.&#xd;
The definitive version was published in PLoS Biology 3 (2005): e144, doi:10.1371/journal.pbio.0030144.</dc:description>
<dc:description>The oceanic cyanobacteria Prochlorococcus are globally important, ecologically diverse primary producers. It is thought&#xd;
that their viruses (phages) mediate population sizes and affect the evolutionary trajectories of their hosts. Here we&#xd;
present an analysis of genomes from three Prochlorococcus phages: a podovirus and two myoviruses. The morphology,&#xd;
overall genome features, and gene content of these phages suggest that they are quite similar to T7-like (P-SSP7) and&#xd;
T4-like (P-SSM2 and P-SSM4) phages. Using the existing phage taxonomic framework as a guideline, we examined&#xd;
genome sequences to establish ‘‘core’’ genes for each phage group. We found the podovirus contained 15 of 26 core&#xd;
T7-like genes and the two myoviruses contained 43 and 42 of 75 core T4-like genes. In addition to these core genes,&#xd;
each genome contains a significant number of ‘‘cyanobacterial’’ genes, i.e., genes with significant best BLAST hits to&#xd;
genes found in cyanobacteria. Some of these, we speculate, represent ‘‘signature’’ cyanophage genes. For example, all&#xd;
three phage genomes contain photosynthetic genes (psbA, hliP) that are thought to help maintain host photosynthetic&#xd;
activity during infection, as well as an aldolase family gene (talC) that could facilitate alternative routes of carbon&#xd;
metabolism during infection. The podovirus genome also contains an integrase gene (int) and other features that&#xd;
suggest it is capable of integrating into its host. If indeed it is, this would be unprecedented among cultured T7-like&#xd;
phages or marine cyanophages and would have significant evolutionary and ecological implications for phage and&#xd;
host. Further, both myoviruses contain phosphate-inducible genes (phoH and pstS) that are likely to be important for&#xd;
phage and host responses to phosphate stress, a commonly limiting nutrient in marine systems. Thus, these marine&#xd;
cyanophages appear to be variations of two well-known phages—T7 and T4—but contain genes that, if functional,&#xd;
reflect adaptations for infection of photosynthetic hosts in low-nutrient oceanic environments.</dc:description>
<dc:description>This research&#xd;
was supported by the US DOE under grant numbers DEFG02–&#xd;
99ER62814 and DE-FG02–02ER63445, and the National Science&#xd;
Foundation under grant number OCE-9820035 (to SWC).</dc:description>

<dc:title>Markov chain analysis of succession in a rocky subtidal community</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Hill, M. Forrest</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Witman, Jon D.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Caswell, Hal</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Markov chain</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Sensitivity analysis</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Transition matrix</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Species diversity</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Entropy</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Dobrushin’s coefficient</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Author Posting. © University of Chicago Press, 2004.  This article is posted here by permission of University of Chicago Press for personal use, not for redistribution.  The definitive version was published in American Naturalist 164 (2004): E46-E61.</dc:description>
<dc:description>We present a Markov chain model of succession in a&#xd;
rocky subtidal community based on a long-term (1986–1994) study&#xd;
of subtidal invertebrates (14 species) at Ammen Rock Pinnacle in&#xd;
the Gulf of Maine. The model describes successional processes (disturbance,&#xd;
colonization, species persistence, and replacement), the&#xd;
equilibrium (stationary) community, and the rate of convergence.&#xd;
We described successional dynamics by species turnover rates, recurrence&#xd;
times, and the entropy of the transition matrix. We used&#xd;
perturbation analysis to quantify the response of diversity to successional&#xd;
rates and species removals. The equilibrium community&#xd;
was dominated by an encrusting sponge (Hymedesmia) and a bryozoan&#xd;
(Crisia eburnea). The equilibrium structure explained 98% of&#xd;
the variance in observed species frequencies. Dominant species have&#xd;
low probabilities of disturbance and high rates of colonization and&#xd;
persistence. On average, species turn over every 3.4 years. Recurrence&#xd;
times varied among species (7–268 years); rare species had the longest&#xd;
recurrence times. The community converged to equilibrium quickly&#xd;
(9.5 years), as measured by Dobrushin’s coefficient of ergodicity. The&#xd;
largest changes in evenness would result from removal of the dominant&#xd;
sponge Hymedesmia. Subdominant species appear to increase&#xd;
evenness by slowing the dominance of Hymedesmia. Comparison of&#xd;
the subtidal community with intertidal and coral reef communities&#xd;
revealed that disturbance rates are an order of magnitude higher in&#xd;
coral reef than in rocky intertidal and subtidal communities. Colonization&#xd;
rates and turnover times, however, are lowest and longest in coral reefs, highest and shortest in intertidal communities, and&#xd;
intermediate in subtidal communities.</dc:description>
<dc:description>This research was supported&#xd;
by National Science Foundation grants DEB-9527400, OCE-981267, OCE-9302238, and OCE-0083976&#xd;
and by the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration’s&#xd;
National Undersea Research Program, University&#xd;
of Connecticut—Avery Point.</dc:description>

</metadata></record><record><header><identifier>oai:darchive.mblwhoilibrary.org:1912/119</identifier><datestamp>2016-03-08T18:01:03Z</datestamp><setSpec>com_1912_3</setSpec><setSpec>col_1912_60</setSpec></header><metadata><oai_dc:dc xmlns:oai_dc="http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc/" xmlns:doc="http://www.lyncode.com/xoai" xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance" xmlns:dc="http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/" xsi:schemaLocation="http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc/ http://www.openarchives.org/OAI/2.0/oai_dc.xsd">
<dc:title>Gene fusions and gene duplications : relevance to genomic annotation and functional analysis</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Serres, Margrethe H.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Riley, Monica</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Escherichia coli</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Multimodular proteins</dc:subject>
<dc:description>© 2005 Serres and Riley.&#xd;
This is an Open Access article distributed under the terms of the Creative Commons Attribution License, which permits unrestricted use, distribution, and reproduction in any medium, provided the original work is properly cited.&#xd;
The definitive version was published in BMC Genomics 6 (2005): 33, doi:10.1186/1471-2164-6-33.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Background: Escherichia coli a model organism provides information for annotation of other&#xd;
genomes. Our analysis of its genome has shown that proteins encoded by fused genes need special&#xd;
attention. Such composite (multimodular) proteins consist of two or more components (modules)&#xd;
encoding distinct functions. Multimodular proteins have been found to complicate both annotation&#xd;
and generation of sequence similar groups. Previous work overstated the number of multimodular&#xd;
proteins in E. coli. This work corrects the identification of modules by including sequence&#xd;
information from proteins in 50 sequenced microbial genomes.&#xd;
Results: Multimodular E. coli K-12 proteins were identified from sequence similarities between&#xd;
their component modules and non-fused proteins in 50 genomes and from the literature. We found&#xd;
109 multimodular proteins in E. coli containing either two or three modules. Most modules had&#xd;
standalone sequence relatives in other genomes. The separated modules together with all the single&#xd;
(un-fused) proteins constitute the sum of all unimodular proteins of E. coli. Pairwise sequence&#xd;
relationships among all E. coli unimodular proteins generated 490 sequence similar, paralogous&#xd;
groups. Groups ranged in size from 92 to 2 members and had varying degrees of relatedness among&#xd;
their members. Some E. coli enzyme groups were compared to homologs in other bacterial&#xd;
genomes.&#xd;
Conclusion: The deleterious effects of multimodular proteins on annotation and on the formation&#xd;
of groups of paralogs are emphasized. To improve annotation results, all multimodular proteins in&#xd;
an organism should be detected and when known each function should be connected with its&#xd;
location in the sequence of the protein. When transferring functions by sequence similarity,&#xd;
alignment locations must be noted, particularly when alignments cover only part of the sequences,&#xd;
in order to enable transfer of the correct function. Separating multimodular proteins into module&#xd;
units makes it possible to generate protein groups related by both sequence and function, avoiding&#xd;
mixing of unrelated sequences. Organisms differ in sizes of groups of sequence-related proteins. A&#xd;
sample comparison of orthologs to selected E. coli paralogous groups correlates with known&#xd;
physiological and taxonomic relationships between the organisms.</dc:description>
<dc:description>The research was supported by the Office of Science&#xd;
(BER), U.S. Department of Energy, Grant No. DE-FG02-01ER63202 and by&#xd;
National Aeronautics and Space Administration Astrobiology grant NCC2-1054.</dc:description>

<dc:title>Structural changes in gill DNA reveal the effects of contaminants on Puget Sound fish</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Malins, Donald C.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Stegeman, John J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Anderson, Jack W.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Johnson, Paul M.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Gold, Jordan</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Anderson, Katie M.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Polynuclear aromatic hydrocarbons (PAH)</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Polychlorinated biphenyls (PCBs)</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>DNA markers</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Contaminant effects,</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Cytochrome P450 1A</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>DNA structure</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Fish gills</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Fourier transform-infrared spectroscopy</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Author Posting. EHP is a publication of the United States government. Publication of EHP lies in the public domain and is therefore without copyright. The definitive version was published in Environmental Health Perspectives 112 (2004): 511-515, doi:10.1289/ehp.6719.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Structural differences were identified in gill DNA from two groups of English sole collected from&#xd;
Puget Sound, Washington, in October 2000. One group was from the industrialized Duwamish&#xd;
River (DR) in Seattle and the other from relatively clean Quartermaster Harbor (QMH).&#xd;
Chemical markers of sediment contamination [e.g., polynuclear aromatic hydrocarbons (PAHs)&#xd;
and polychlorinated biphenyls (PCBs)] established that the DR was substantially more contaminated&#xd;
than QMH. The levels of these chemicals in the sediments of both sites were consistent with&#xd;
levels of cytochrome P450 1A (CYP1A) expression in the gills of English sole from the same sites.&#xd;
Structural differences in gill DNA between the groups were evinced via statistical models of Fourier&#xd;
transform-infrared (FT-IR) spectra. Marked structural damage was found in the gill DNA of the&#xd;
DR fish as reflected in differences in base functional groups (e.g., C–O and NH2) and conformational&#xd;
properties (e.g., arising from perturbations in vertical base stacking interactions). These&#xd;
DNA differences were used to discriminate between the two fish groups through principal components&#xd;
analysis of mean FT-IR spectra. In addition, logistic regression analysis allowed for the development&#xd;
of a “DNA damage index” to assess the effects of contaminants on the gill. The evidence&#xd;
implies that environmental chemicals contribute to the DNA changes in the gill. The damaged&#xd;
DNA is a promising marker for identifying, through gill biopsies, contaminant effects on fish.</dc:description>
<dc:description>This publication was made possible by grant P42&#xd;
ES04696 (D.C.M.) and 5-P42-ESO7381 (J.J.S.)&#xd;
from the National Institute of Environmental Health&#xd;
Sciences (NIEHS), National Institutes of Health&#xd;
(NIH).</dc:description>

<dc:title>Environmental health and the coastal zone</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Stegeman, John J.</dc:creator>
<dc:creator>Solow, Andrew R.</dc:creator>
<dc:subject>Human health</dc:subject>
<dc:subject>Coastal zone</dc:subject>
<dc:description>Author Posting. EHP is a publication of the United States government. Publication of EHP lies in the public domain and is therefore without copyright. The definitive version was published &#xd;
in Environmental Health Perspectives 110 (2002):  A660-A661.</dc:description>
<dc:description>Reconciling coastal development and the maintenance of a quality&#xd;
environment represent an enormous management challenge to both&#xd;
public and private interests. Wise management of coastal areas will&#xd;
require an understanding of the nature of dynamic physical, chemical,&#xd;
and biological interactions in the coastal zone, knowledge of how&#xd;
changes in other components of the Earth system affect coastal zones&#xd;
and their role in global cycles, and insight into how to best use these&#xd;
areas as coastal populations increase. Maintaining the integrity and&#xd;
health of the coastal zone is essential to the quality of marine biological&#xd;
resources and, ultimately, of human life.</dc:description>
